Background

Open-source solutions have emerged, but it has not been as hot as since new LLM
Experience with ML Ops, AI models, using/buying training data
It all comes down to data. Data is the foundation for all of this. It’s vital to have either really clean and good data from the get-go, or really robust ETL pipelines.
I’ve worked in environments where data stored in warehouse (Redshit, Big Query, [ORG]); sometimes in cloud storage ([ORG] S3); then how data scientists interact with the data (Jupiter Notebook: write queries, explore the data, mark down visuals; or querying directly to a database; locally or in cloud); deploying in production
Emerging them over last 3 years: ensuring continued success in terms of that model. Continuous training (based on new data), metrics analysis, A/B testing
Outsource vs in-house?
Vendors tend to happen further upstream in working with business stakeholders to identify use cases. At [ORG], we did an internal valuation of a specific personalization type of vendor focused on content. Opted to build a model internally (like POC with this vendor), deployed them concurrently and looked at results.
Other area for vendors: a couple years ago, focused on specific imaging problems (deep-learning, CV-type problems). Went with a vendor for image classification: easier to take their off-the-shelf model and deploy it against our data. Came down to expertise
For the most part, we do things in-house. Work with vendors for data. [Ask [PERSON] if still need to ask about lifecycle]
Purchasing process and [ORG]; budget
My experience has been a lot of personalization, advertising and marketing technology (optimize how money is spent in terms of acquiring users and monetizing users with ad-supported content); also engaging customers ([ORG])
Marketing is usually the stakeholders [[PERSON]: starts with Business]. One of two ways: engage my team (IT org) and want to do a POC with this type of company. Or come to IT with a problem: IT evaluates the problem and evaluates whether it should be done internally or with a vendor.
Once [ORG], some degree of discovery for vendor. Discussing data to extent necessary: what data is available to them, data privacy agreements. Agreement of POC scope: how much data, how long they have to deploy solution. Usually happens concurrently with more than one vendor, maybe two. It often happens in organizations, 50-75% of time: just a good practice to benchmark, even if decision has already been made (validate the decision). With LLM ([ORG] vs [ORG]), orgs already have ties to the disperse ecosystems and lean into where they have relationships).
Decision-makers: Marketing leadership, in some cases I was ultimate decision-maker and others part of the panel, and executive sponsors.
Who is called for POC: depends on how engagement occurs (driven by IT or Marketing). Always a buy vs build decision: you go with vendors for maintainability, responsibility (easier to shift responsibility to a vendor), cost is a consideration, time to market (esp for marketing), business impact/ROI
[ORG]: data quality is important and probably top of list; that includes data accuracy. Are there APIs and workflows that can be automated (one-time setup cost but will make our lives easier). Existing relationships are important: we worked with [ORG]; 
Relationships: somebody knows someone at the company, or has worked with them before. In other cases, broader executive charter: we go to these vendors for these types of things. It’s pretty sporadic: not a well-[ORG], quantifiable way with relationships. It also comes down to value: large companies have a lot of red tape to onboard new vendors (significantly decreases the opportunity to fish in the open market for new vendors; but in some cases it makes sense if they really have an edge)
Compare [ORG] (tech startup) with [ORG] (enterprise) vs non-tech ([ORG])
Different buying behavior, depending on corporations’ value. [ORG]’s #1 value is trust: they put that ahead of everything else. [ORG] is much smaller, capable of moving faster and can take on more risk with the hope of rewards. [ORG] is somewhere in between (mid-enterprise): straddles trust (multi-billion dollar org) but willing to bear certain risks.
[ORG] would do things themselves or broker an engagement. I ended up at [ORG] by being a customer at [ORG]
The larger the company is, the more capital they have. [ORG] is in the realm of several millions to tens of millions. [ORG] a few millions: 1-10 million. Startup company: less than $1 m
Smaller companies are more sensitive to cost and value. Can be pre-revenue / trying to monetize. Privacy/security: less mature in those domains (no privacy counsel).
How important are relationships? What does it take to start one or get kicked off?
How important is work surface / technology? Key characteristics
VPC (like a VPN): if you have a cloud footprint (like [ORG]), can create controls around VPC and even further VPN. Security gates you put around your environment. Cloud-forward companies (most of my experience): the goal is how to use this technology within our own VPC. Bringing the technology (through APIs, SDKs) into our ecosystem; or VPC-pairing (my cloud ecosystem is still mine, but create a relationship between our systems that allows for auditing, for example).
We want our vendors to bring their own tools, in all cases ([ORG], [ORG], [ORG]).
[[PERSON]: Do you expect vendors to do pre-processing?] Usually the customer does the pre-processing? In some cases, customer builds own ETL. Depends on use case. Usually the vendor will manage. Purchasing process
For known problems (updated yearly), procurement keeps a shortlist of preferred vendors (or exclusion criteria for vendors). For example [ORG] dataset on a drug – I contact [ORG] directly (1-2 vendor, get a quote and go to procurement). Takes 1 month
If I know a solution exists, but don’t know who has the data, we have an idea of 3-4 vendors: may reach out to them directly. We select best 2 quotes, and go to procurement with these quotes. 1-3 months.
If I don’t know whether a solution exists in the market, I trigger an RFP (separate process, 5-10 vendors): give vendors a task. Typical RFP takes 3-6 months: we prefer using another process. We prefer to refine the problem definition internally, so we get a sense of who the vendors are (1-3 months)

Demand by data type
Audio: there is quite low demand. Already good speech-to-text and text-to-speech solutions: solved problem. Model would transcribe our call, then summarize the content from the text (not the audio). Voice assistant and music are the market ([ORG], iTunes). 
McK put out report saying $5 trillion in market opportunity by enhancing productivity in the workforce – they all use text).
Images and videos: interesting. [ORG] Photoshop AI assistant, really popular. You need annotators / graphic designers (e.g. paint image in a night scene). Think both have a substantial market, but smaller than text.
Language/text: primary thing everyone uses, all knowledge workers. Communication over text: spoken or written. Sales outreach – how do you automate this with generative. Will grow the fastest, probably also the biggest. 99% of knowledge workers primarily use language/text, 1% are graphic/game designers / video producers.
Market by geography
In terms of AI, leading markets are North American and China. US is multiple times bigger than European market. US companies have higher risk tolerance. Really strong US bias. Most companies focus on US market and English language. China is more isolated, with Chinese companies.
Spend on AI and data for AI (highly correlated): 80-90% is US, Europe 10%, Latam/Africa: close to 0%. JP (more ambitious, become an AI leader, changing the law to make environment AI-friendly)/SK (1-2%): single digits
Spend by AI startups
Jasper: designing interface around [ORG], but not training own data models. 
Most of the startups are wrappers around [ORG], [ORG], [ORG]. Developing these foundation models goes in hundreds of millions in cost, or billions for next generation of models. Not feasible for most companies.
Use API to a foundation model, don’t do much fine-tuning? You don’t see that many fine-tune models. Their value-add is on the usability. 
Prompt engineering: what these models can do, taking longer instructions in.

Jasper figures out how to make it easy for people in copy-writing to use. They did not do anything on the model: integrate on your browser, and doing prompt engineering – giving the right commands.
Got disrupted by [ORG], which was really easy to use. Anyone can log in: ask for 100-word product description. Biggest competitor to Jasper.

Annual spend (well more than 100 million; [ORG] spends well north of $100 million per year, maybe up to $1 billion): how has it evolved and how will it evolve?
It’s been going up. I’ve only been here 18 months. Growing maybe 20% a year; systems are more efficient so need less data, but new needs emerge
More data annotation than collection; sometimes companies do both. 30% collection, 70% annotation, or maybe 40/60.
Across [ORG] as a whole: bulk is in visual data, and maybe video. More than 50% video, then image, then speech, then audio. Also product use cases, like ranking use cases. More and more, it’s multi-modal learning, so need all modalities together.
So many computer vision use cases: metaverse use cases (build avatars), make a video
Usually vendors are very specialized, and often have their own tooling. We typically have them work on one mode. Purchasing Behavior
Key Purchasing Criteria
First we need to have confidence that vendor can do the task we need them to do, and do it well. Comes from referrals, talking to vendors. Can they do video, speech, etc.
Then are they able to deal with our [ORG]. We want large datasets: not every vendor is staffed to do that.
Another one would be quality and turnaround time. Do a pilot with the vendor, often flawed because vendors do a better job in pilot than production
Then cost, but not dominant factor at [ORG]. We try to negotiate a good deal, because we’re a big name.
Another important aspect is data handling and compliance of vendor: are they taking best practices of data encryption, location. Also regulation for data collection efforts.

What do you guys do and what do you use AI/ML and training data for?

Vendors
We ended up evaluating [ORG] and [ORG]. We looked at those two and did not. Went with [ORG] and [ORG], hired contractors (through agency) to do all our labeling. 
We were ahead of the curve on data labeling, way before it blew up with [ORG].
With [ORG], we didn’t trust their workforce: random people off the internet as far as we can tell, though they put a lot of time into QC. Didn’t want to trust our labeling with, because such . Also train our models on data that is proprietary: how do we sign NDA, etc. Just seemed like a lot of work. Also more control, including on labeling guidelines. We needed to label 10 m data points to train these models, not massive. [ORG] would get us 500 contractors: we’re not that big.
We didn’t even get to pricing conversation: not a good fit
[ORG]: we weren’t impressed with their product 2 years ago. Rules-based systems to pre-label data. It’s an afternoon job for a data scientist on my team.
If you have the expertise in-house, always be a better job.

100% text. [ORG] enables us to do nice custom interfaces. [ORG] was more into self-driving cars. Their portfolio has evolved though, so we may evaluate them again this year. Use cases
For semantic search, we do entailment: used everywhere in our search product. Provide pairs of sentences of that mean same thing and pairs of sentences that don’t mean the same thing.
[ORG] is publicly-traded, not a startup
If you’re starting off on a small project, it would be a bold bet to hire someone to manage the labeling project. Most people running startups don’t really think about details, they’re product-oriented. Once you have a decent model, need to pay a lot of attention
Any startup that has a high amount of data, more likely to do this stuff in-house.
No shortage of accuracy problems with GPT4, which hallucinates. It’s because of how they chose to label, via [ORG]’s distributed workforce.
If you’re in regulated industry, like finance or law, you can’t be taking those kinds of risks. Can’t ask [ORG] to do it.
We build both from scratch and use. Three options – we have models in all 3. Since we’re in digital experience, we really want it closely linked to the brand voice. We want stuff that’s easily retrainable. Easiest to use open-source model and fine-tuning: that’s most of what we do. You get 20-30% improved at 1/10th of the cost (compared with third-party hosted service) – but you need people who know what they’re doing. [ORG] is good enough for an MVP

Upskilling a workforce – why so important?
People talk a lot in this space about error rate. Cutting edge doesn’t care about 95-99%, but care about quality of work on the ones where you’re making mistakes. Work upstream to set up correct definitions to get best output, then highly consistent workforce working on same set of data for multiple years (highly engaged); real-time feedback from workforce. Background
Spent last 4 years at [ORG], in a variety of roles. Most recently [ORG] of [ORG] (data annotation software platform). Historically, most of [ORG]’s business is around E2E data annotation: customers provide raw, unlabelled data. We label data and send them back to them. Product guardrails, ML

Market segmentation, size, growth, profitability, trends
Market segmentation
Data annotation – best way to segment it is by data modality. Primary fork is computer vision vs text.
Within CV, a few sub-segments: image annotation (most common one), closely followed by video. Less common: 3D [ORG]/sensor fusion, vastly for self-driving (no other applications). Create ground truth data to train models and test models they create on subset of data: bifurcating dataset as 80% training and 20% testing.
Within text, a few areas in last 4 years: 
Historically primarily comprised of traditional NLP (highlight words in a text: subject, sentiment; use to train NLP NER models) and document extraction (hybrid between CV and text: taking a PDF doc of bill of lading, extract info from doc; both CV element – position on page – and text extraction)
In last 6 months, starting in Nov 2022: text annotation to support LLM building. Use technique RLHF (reinforcement learning with human feedback): customer would provide with model outputs from LLM, we would use humans to provide feedback to indicate better response. Iteratively train model. Seen different variations of that: rate response 1-5, if 3 or below can ask human to make corrections (customer feeds response back to model). Taking model responses and seeing 1/ if sensitive information, 2/ if bias in response. Gaining relevance in industry, with LLM trust and safety.
[Prompted] Audio labeling: we saw a bit of that, but not as much. Sub-categories: transcription (taking a pure audio file and transcribing it into text), sometimes a second step and analyzing the text. Historically, another type is audio quality: rate call recording quality from 1-5. Less frequent. From our experience, audio to text transcriptions is a semi-solved problem. [ORG]’s Whisper is open MIT license: quite good already. Less common to take audio to text from scratch; more common to review model outputs. A few providers do this type of work: [ORG] is pretty large in this space (specialized in audio to text: pure humans, model-driven). [Prompted] Training Siri and Alexa is not a super large market: companies doing audio assistant work (Siri, Alexa, [ORG] Home), have so much concern about leaking audio files, do primarily in-house. Quite rare for a third-party to work with that data. Even if provide to third-party, may anonymize or modify voice pitch.
Market split by input type: rapidly shifting. Back in 2019, I would say: 80-90% is under computer vision (with a big part for sensor fusion: expensive to annotate and a lot of money poured into that sector). Now: much larger portion is within text, very much driven by LLM work. Arms race between foundational ([ORG], [ORG], [ORG] + other players trying to train own LLMs on top of open source models). 40% is around text / LLM / RLHF labeling. Remaining 60% is computer vision: breakdown within CV is similar to 2019, 50-60% of CV dollars are in sensor fusion, another 30% is video, 20% image. This is for external, third-party spend. External spend on audio: Rev has $100 m in annual revenue, but don’t know about larger size; audio to drive ML models. Total external could be $200 m, but I’m not sure. [[PERSON]: we do $12 m in audio for Siri]
Question around what is pure content moderation vs content moderation to feed into LLM trust and safety model. [ORG] used and dropped [ORG], or other BPOs. Trust and safety is a large category of spend for – not included in 100% pie, because no visibility.
Market size overall: pretty large market that is continuing to grow. Best pulse on total third-party market size. Definitely within the billions, single or double digit billions. I wouldn’t be surprised if it were low double-digit billions. 
Segment industry players:
incumbents like [ORG], [ORG] – more old school players. Based on public revenue disclosures, kind of plateau-ing – because newer players like [ORG], [ORG] or others who are rapidly growing. Taking share from those players and capturing new spend.
Also decent long-tail of providers: if international or price-sensitive, may go to a BPO in Philippines or Africa. Larger companies doing high volume.
[Prompted on size beyond $ 1 billion] From my experience, there’s a decent amount of non-traditional spend, like BPOs. Data services – a lot of potential where [ORG] may not play today. One area where [ORG] had not done much in but investing now: e-commerce data cleaning. Is it true text vs image, more ambiguous? An area that we hadn’t considered extensively, but a lot of spend behind that. If go to e-commerce page, click on item: description, tags (color, pattern, attributes). A lot of these catalogs are messy, esp if third-party marketplace (sellers may not have great data integrity, may not provide accurate form-fitting data into listing). If you have a better catalog, can directly attribute that to revenue / conversion uplift (3-5% - pretty significant for most e-commerce websites, though unclear how much labeling is needed): better searchability, better UX, more customer trust. Better search ranking, can use side-bar filters. [ORG] has a business plan around that. 
Most players, except top 3-5 players, don’t have the capability to do output training. Mostly catalog cleanup.
[[PERSON] asked about search relevance] Search relevance falls into data labeling. What do customers do with it? Primary bifurcation: train/improve/evaluate model, or put it live into production? 
Margin profile between labeling and data cleanup
We thought about margin as follows: primary aspect driving gross margin is level of human involvement, and how qualified need to be. Drawing a box around an image vs rank LLM output on quality (and rewrite if quality is below 3 – requires good human cognition). Amount of time spent, type of person needed (offshore vs onshore); depending on use case, can be ML pre-labeling (more common in computer vision). Hosting, processing costs are pretty minor.
Typical margin trajectory: when new customer, margin (universal) starts low (trying to ramp quickly, iterating with customer on instructions) and over time you can optimize costs.
Tech-enabled data service providers like [ORG]. In self-driving, most customers are interested in annotating the same thing (people, bikes, etc.). Pre-labeling depends on the industry / use case: let’s see if we can use a foundational model, then optimize that or provide humans.
[[PERSON]: how much pre-sale consulting is possible?] I do think that [ORG] is unique in its ability to do so. [ORG] hires impressive folks, technically strong. [ORG]: more old school, less technical expertise – lower level of consultation of pushback. [ORG]: esp in self-driving, we hired a lot of people in that industry, so we were able to share best practices, though don’t always listen, pretty mixed. What customers believe to be optimal: because closely guarded (secret sauce – many customers very protective about instruction sets and taxonomy – don’t want competitors to know what they label), but sometimes kind of lost so we consult. The earlier the industry, the more a player like [ORG] can help shape customer outcome (e.g. early days of LLM, more receptiveness from customers). Customers don’t often know what they want: may still be edge cases or customers change their mind. Customers need to see annotated data, before ramp up to production levels.

[[PERSON]: can [ORG] compete with Indian BPO? Long-term economic model for this industry; talent model changes?] Operating profile [ORG] by two aspects: consultative aspect (tech-savvy: high-quality EM). Also, Indian BPO does not have pre-labeling (product and technology benefits) + additional consultative layer – customers are in good hands. Tech benefits are pretty persistent: unlikely than an Indian BPO can create a pre-labeling model (why?). Our customers value technical expertise, and not necessarily very price-sensitive. But our selection is biased: I see folks who work with us. Will be ongoing: always advancements in AI. Frontier-pushing customers will work with [ORG]: sharpen their thinking, can [ORG] early on. Maybe over time, as downward price pressure and commoditization. Or our pre-labeling becomes so good that we have software-like margins and we’re competitive on price. Waves of new AI advancement: always room for a frontier.

Baseline comparison: traditional BPO, much thinner margins. 10-20-30% if they optimize.
It’s mostly driven by tech optimization. If the task is extracting text fields from PDF docs: traditional BPO vendor has humans do 100% extraction; depending on customer quality need, may have additional QA layers; increasing the cost by 70-100%; 
If tech-driven approach, do it manually initially; then take extracted output to train ML model; then does first pass, and you have a cheaper QA layer. How you get from 20% to 70%
Other than [ORG], one vendor who has good tech optimization is [ORG] (more present in text than CV); more geared towards NLP and RLHF. In CV, collection is still a fraction. Use cases would be AR/VR. Self-driving companies collect their own data. Medical providers also provide own data.

Customers don’t bifurcate; do training and evaluation themselves. 80/20 split, or 70/30 or 90/10 split.

Levers for margin improvement
Margin / cost benchmark. Margin depends on volume, project complexity (plain English – I would charge a lot more ; gross margin is higher on higher complexity / lower volume projects)

Standard high volume sensor fusion project: 40-50% contribution margin (excluding R&D but including other indirect costs, such as management overhead, office, software), [ORG] of 60-70%. Overhead cost in the industry are 
Major buckets of costs and how to optimize
Software / tools used / hosting / platform management: 
Acquisition and payment of labelers (high turnover): can be 1:1, or 20:10
On highly standardized projects, gross margins are 40%. 15% labeler, 15% software
On super complex, gross margins are 70%. 25% is labelers
When project is commoditized and large volume, customers are very cost-sensitive (millions of dollars). If it’s a one-time project or very difficult, less price-sensitive because few options. One of my customers, I charged them twice next best option
Levers to improve margin - Only three options: specialize in niche areas, dramatically cut costs or take on challenging customers (e.g. large automotive groups in the laggard category: not many people bid on their projects)
Our biggest revenue was coming from products. I cut down acquisition costs: I paid people more on higher retention, and spent less on CAC (labeler acquisition). Training is expensive, acquisition is even more expensive: most expensive part is the ramp up. Proficiency leads to higher accuracy and speed. Paying people more leads to better margins
Is customer concentration inevitable?
Large tech companies are great to work with. They understand the technicality and the value of the project. Just want to make the project work. Becomes very reciprocal. One customer came to us and I needed to duplicate our company (in volume) in 3 months: they value the relationship, our flexibility, that we don’t hold them to their letter (figuring this out together). We more than 10x their volume in 3 months, and quality dropped 10 bps below agreement; they didn’t care.

Iw as in charge of determining cost, complexity and pricing. Arabic 25-30% premium. Swedish closer to 2x gross margin (if English was 60%, Spanish was 60-65%, Swedish was 75-80%).

Churn factors changed a lot by region: community support was important, pay not as much as you think (had bumps – above a certain limit, people would stay but would not increase likeliness to stay until to determine that plateau; figure it out via AB testing; I constantly had 10-20 experiments running – that’s what we did at [ORG] that others didn’t, understanding how market works)
We chose not to engage in projects where we only provide workforce: I cannot control quality or protect my name. In all our big tech projects, we controlled the platform.
Finding the right workforce, keep them engaged and effective is a very complicated problem. Solution is that 1/ recognize it’s complex, 2/ have knowledge and skills, including data, to solve it (e.g. correlation between pay/retention, pay/quality, size of community/retention & satisfaction; figuring out which analytics to look after, and 3/ really get ready to get hands and feet dirty, go to a lot of places you didn’t think

Can you to speak to who the main types of customers are for AI data services? What is the most relevant way of segmenting customers (e.g. by industry, by size, by other criteria), and why?
The main customers for AI Data Services are (1) any customer that has a sizeable IT/HR help desk with a large headcount, (2) any customer that has a sizeable customer support service desk, (3) any customer with a sizeable in-house Digital and SW Engineering staff, (4) any client that is in the publishing, marketing, and ad campaign business, (5) any software firm that is in the CRM, CSM, ITSM, HRSM, IVR business. Can you also speak to what buyers’ most important criteria are when deciding which provider(s) to purchase AI training data from? Does this vary by data type, buyer size and/or industry? Please briefly elaborate.
When deciding which provider(s) to purchase AI training data from, buyers consider several important criteria. While these criteria can vary based on data type, buyer size, and industry, some common factors influence the decision-making process. These are some key considerations: Data Quality and Accuracy, Data Relevance and Diversity, Data Annotation and Labeling, Scalability and Volume, Data Security and Privacy, Cost and Pricing Model, Industry Expertise and Domain Knowledge, Customizability and Flexibility, Data Licensing and Intellectual Property and Reputation and Customer References.

June 26, 2023 follow-up call


Notes
Data collection: does it matter more for certain customers than other?
For us, data collection is a small part of our business, but large segment of demand in the industry as a whole. We are seeing growth – demand is increasing. Depends on the use case. If an org already has a lot of data, don’t necessarily need more. [ORG] does not need more social media data, but do need specialized computer vision data to train metaverse use case which doesn’t exist. Companies may have data but not allowed to share with vendors: need to create data in a compliant manner. Most companies have some degree
It’s possible to just focus on annotation. Collection and annotation is very different in terms of operations: annotation is about tooling and good framework to hire/manage risk around large annotation workforces; collection is more about understanding compliance re privacy laws, and logistical know-how / on-the-ground operations. Entail different sets of muscles; some of our competitors. We try to use combination as a differentiator
[ORG], [ORG]: on a legacy basis, with big tech, they provide both
[ORG]: more and more we see them
Traditional BPOs (e.g. [ORG]): initially got into annotation through existing big tech relationships, starting to see BPO enter data collection. Started from a customer need, blocking their ability to access annotation. Customers ask them to leverage global footprint and operational. Not super complex.
What is a good, profitable type of data collection engagement, vs one you would pass on?
Knowing how competitive and complex data complex has been. 
Most interesting is persistent data collection (not a 4-week project across 20 locales; hard to ramp up/down; how we earned out stripes initially)
Prefer to compete where we can differentiate: complex, multi-locales concurrently. Focus on locales where we have strong footprint. Data collection is focusing more on harder to create data. We like AR/VR because more complex: not so many companies (can count on 1 hand), but if we believe the hype, will be enormously important. Not just functionality, but also bias mitigation. Zuckerberg thinks we will do everything in the [ORG]-verse in 2 years: not sure it will evolve. Maybe 5% of the market; could become 10%. AR/VR have increased significantly in AR/VR for us; maybe similar or smaller than audio in size, but growing faster. Zuckerberg says they will double down, but [ORG] is in their year of efficiency: like a lot of big tech, focus on doing more with less. So might decrease funding elsewhere. [ORG] has choppy investment in AR/VR and sunset [ORG] Glass. [ORG] is in the mix with Vision Pro; if [ORG] is successful will have a big impact on the market
Audio: % of market (by date type) and growth. Solved problem?
Audio: has reached parity. Was a huge focus 5-10 years ago there was a lot of focus on speech. Most of our competitors have focused on image and video. Siris and Alexas will continue to be very important; voice search is critical, plays a key role in multi-modal evolution. Don’t think it’s growth will outpace growth. Will depend on [ORG] and Alexa. Also the gaming industry – has an impact. 10-15% for audio sounds good in terms of audio market size, roughly in line with our business. Was dominant 5-ish years ago for us.
I think audio will grow slower than image/video: that’s been the trend. It’s been a relatively stable growth rate over last 24 months; I would expect it to stay this way. I think audio will grow at 15% per year over next few years, slower than market overall; though I can’t predict voice search (e.g. with gen AI)
Seems like there’s so much to do in computer vision.
Text had been declining, and it’s a huge focus now with generative AI, and interaction of gen AI with search. Need for high-quality, expert-annotated and created content. This year, text growth is outstripping what we expected because of [ORG] and its competitors. Don’t know how long this current rush will continue, and at what level will it slow down. I’ve heard 1-2 years, or continuous: expectation of performance will continue to increase. I think it will be 3+ years.
In-house vs outsource: what dynamics, by type of player?
Many large tech companies and AV customers do prefer to outsource annotation; often large workforces are required, which I assume comes with risk which they don’t want, and large cost (thousands of people).
In-house is not unheard of; some big tech companies maintain large teams overseas, but it’s less common
We’ve seen some increase in demand for in-facility workforces, driven by a few things.
Skepticism / concern about crowd model: confidentiality, risk of co-employment, PII-related. Increase in distrust of contractor model.
For our business, in-facility is 30% and it is increasing. Not just large tech – also automotive, healthcare. We’ve seen increased interest across verticals. If sensitive content, or PII, or prototype-related.
Initially for us, it was the exception
Sub-contracting options are available as well, but favors large players somewhat.
Sales by channels: % of sales?
I don’t know market %. Indirect is very insignificant for my company, and relatively insignificant for our direct competitors. Might be more important for smaller or niche players. That is on our radar as a channel for us. Not significant yet: a bit less attractive because disintermediated from customer. Selection is often price-driven and transactional. It could become significant in the future, with LLM work. Enterprise customers may look to procure their datasets for now. Such a period of growth over last 5-10 years.
We might see data platforms as partners; something we’re evaluating now. A couple of our direct competitors (could be [ORG] or [ORG]) have announced a burgeoning partnership with some of these players. A bit of overlap, so some competitive complexity to navigate.
[ORG] has announced a SaaS partnership with [ORG] ([ORG] platform opening them up to [ORG] customers), not service.
Customer segments
[Prompted] Mega tech, autonomous driving, large tech, non-tech enterprise, tech startups
I agree with your hierarchy (in terms of market size). Gov is a large segment you didn’t mention, with its own unique buying behavior.
Role of procurement within big tech: much more organized and military-industrial complex-like because of spend level. Less formal, once you get done to bottom of hierarchy: we see more churn, e.g. in startups. A lot of startups coming and going, budget issues, smaller projects, more iterative projects because strategy is evolving. Big tech is more investment upstream, so more prescriptive asks, less churn on budget and scope change.
More programmatic demand with big tech, and a decent amount of experimentation. 50-60% of spend is ongoing programs on refining. Ad hoc could be a year-long data collection project, or on-going LLM training. LLM training was not ongoing until this year. If we include LLM training, could be 60-75% ongoing. We’re seeing a pooling of spend
More volatile as you go down hierarchy
Gov: becoming an approved supplier is a specific framework, but actual demand behaves as Big Tech

Offerings by segment
Large tech – what do they buy?
Most frequently annotation. What is a bit unique to that segment, unlike the big 5, may not have proprietary task management / annotation platform, frequently interested in a SaaS solution with a partner, possibly multi-vendor solution so that they can deploy a vendor platform in a SaaS model without being tied to one vendor.
In some cases, data collection too.
Large tech companies and non-tech enterprise frequently have not invested in their own platform. Need to work with a platform provider: can be a pure-play or services provider with a SaaS offering
Sole partner, e.g. [ORG] is your partner, using [ORG] as your platform
Or multi-vendor platform
Big tech generally prefers to use their own platform for labeling: it’s multi-vendor, gives them more predictability on price, more control
Market size by offering
[Prompted] Thoughts on the following split&
Relevance work: 30-40% of market?
Computer vision: 30-40%
Everything else: 30%
Text and audio annotation: 15%
Data generation: 15%
I think this split is directionally accurate. The big increase in demand for high-quality LLM datasets has shifted things a bit; could be data generation or annotation, includes things like prompt engineering. Started to take some share from. Spend on LLM work is a moving target; some of these companies in a competitive race are pursuing an “all hands on deck, code red” approach. My sense is that they’re declining their focus on core products to fund LLM initiatives. Lines are blurring in these offerings, e.g. between LLM and search for companies offering both
LLM offerings: from prompt engineering/writing, response writing, response re-writing, annotation, validation, relevance work around LLM work. We’ve seen most demand in the first half: prompt writing, response writing. Also demand in technical content (math, coding) and some non-tech content: building the responses
Historically annotation has not been a specialized profile: representative profile that can be trained. LLM has been a big focus this year, and to some extent starting last year. Now looking at PhDs in calculus, lawyers. Not sure when market growth will slow and LLM enter maintenance mode.
For what % of work and customer types is multi-lingual, multi-local a differentiator?
English vs non-English market %
English is still very dominant, including in LLM dataset. Race is to achieve dominance in English. Multi-lingual is important for big tech, multi-market: big part of big tech strategy, but deprioritized in case of LLM work. Try to get to top 20-40 markets, as quickly as possible after English.
For text and audio, 60% English. Just text is 80% English.
Trends
Impact on market growth of
Automation: it’s evolving in real-time. I think the impact will be significant, but we’re still early in that shift. Technology / AI has not yielded credible results until results: personally, unofficially, will have a very disruptive impact. Today low-quality options like M Turk and other open crowd models, then supposedly high-quality options like [ORG] (frequently using a crowd, though highly-trained, bespoke, curated). Maybe less investment in the middle ground like what we specialize in, and more focus on high-end (specialized/expert rater: high confidence, high trust model). We haven’t seen buying behavior shift yet. We’re helping the disruptors, but could end up being disrupted ourselves
I think market growth will be 30-50%. This year will be very interesting, given influx in demand to support LLM dataset needs.
AI in productivity tools: it’s not insignificant, it’s been somewhat stable, might increase slightly. Ability to enhance and differentiate core products with AI. We’ve seen a bit of enhanced investments

One of the things that triggered the business changing started in the audio space. Whisper came out (general purpose LLM): really good out of the box at recognizing and transcribing short/long format audio. 2012-2016: we were doing a lot of language model development and needed great data. We’d ask [ORG] to annotate 30-50k short audio data (high accuracy speech IVR – how can I help you?) at which company? You wouldn’t ask for it anymore. Whisper came out and a few things after it, including from [ORG]. Technology is good enough to do “few shot learning”: domain adaptation. Instead of 50-100k, need less than 5k, sometimes a few hundred (per language). Good part of the business: people want to do multiple languages, 30-60 languages; [ORG] could deliver for over 200. More diverse set of data that needs to be collected and annotated.
In 2021, people were saying projects coming in were half the size compared to last year. Models out of the box were so good. Got more into specialization: data collection projects are becoming much more specialized, people are becoming matured, people have had AI-based products and services for a few years. Sometimes need to hire out to fill a hole that are really key. Example in image annotation: law enforcement has to recognize bad events that are unusual (person laying on the street), manufacturing (cameras on production site, automated monitoring
Market size
If market/project, one deal at $30 and another deal at $40, and one deal at $20. If you’re used to selling large projects, if qualification criteria were not agile enough, or not good at working with smaller companies that want $20 project. Requires an agility in sales and delivery.
$100 turned into $50-60. Make up for that by charging a bit on pre-labeling, but a lot of competitive pressure globally. 
Average project size went from $100 to $50 between 2020 and 2022.
Not able to make up in value per unit. Instructions to human annotators got more complex. Plenty of people who could follow more complex instructions. Some of the complexity is in data filtering before reaches annotators.
More competitive pressures to take samples, drive up quality, do live quality samplings. Customers don’t come back for business if don’t delivery quality.
Customers expecting more quality for same delivery time and cost. Looking for more specialized / nuanced / thoughtful data annotation. Need passionate annotators who were precise.
If it’s simple and commoditized that can be done in India or Venezuela, getting syphoned off to people who were “butts in seats”, crowdsourced professional services folks with low overhead and limited technology. We were in the space where software could help: software pre-labeling became more of a thing, and became a thing people don’t expect to pay for. Perception of the value is in quality metrics and specialized data collection.
Big tech companies ([ORG], [ORG], [ORG] to a lesser extent, top 5-10 companies) want to control their entire data pipeline: became increasingly harder to integrate [ORG]’s platform. It was very hard to be adaptive enough to all these data pipelines. Their annotation platforms are not more versatile, not that great: just picked the annotation they needed. Productivity, QC metrics were lower or non-existent, but tightly integrated (data comes from our social network, and we can be reactive/quicker to what people talk about today)
Why I left: we’re far too dependent on large companies and not strategic with them. You’re too replaceable. 80%, or 50-60% of revenue: no shot at differentiating on technology. It would always trend down-market towards people who didn’t have sophisticated data pipelines, or could
Individual ability for [ORG] to quantitatively make up for revenue downfall
Big trend for there being a few LLM ([ORG], [ORG], etc.): hundred of billions of parameters. We don’t need to. Like when search hit in the 90s: everyone had a search engine, then the market consolidated.
Market is in the domain-specific language model (what I’m building now since I left [ORG]): people have specialized needs for lower cost model. 
Specialized data loading: when everyone has access to a great LLM, people who will be successful are the ones who have access to rich data in that domain. That’s the future.
Instead of selling to 10 guys for 100 m, need to sell to 1k guys for 1 m. Very difficult to pivot. [ORG]’s ability to do that is too slow: disastrous for certain departments.
Companies that will be successful: can adapt and empower customers to do more things themselves, because they’re the experts in their domain. Data service provider can’t be an expert in every space.
Customers becoming more sophisticated: from outsourcing everything, to being very prescriptive with complex opinions about accuracy, need to read 80 page manual
Market will become more fragmented. Will become more niche competitors. [ORG]: invest a lot of money in a small number of use cases. They correctly identified that they wouldn’t compete with what [ORG] has been good at for 10-12 years (language), so focused on computer vision: their original vision. 2020 was a big year of project for self-driving: looking for specialists in that space.
Pivoting to more fragmented client landscape
Customer empowerment (instructional videos, ability to check their work at the end – sth the customer can do on their own, what happens on the platform, what people see, when can get an error back)
Adaptive data flow in; APIs need to be self-service. Can’t [ORG] ability to integrate yourself, so need to use customers’ developers, need better developer programs.
Let customers design the job, rather than letting you design the job.
I think there was fantastic overspending at [ORG] and [ORG], more than they needed, old-school annotation. Went through a modernization of what they needed; they were overpopulating their model with too much data. More data is not better, higher quality is better

Screening Questions

She has 13 years experience in this space across 2 years with [ORG] and 11 years with [ORG], where she was one of the only people who had multiple roles across the project delivery market. They provide a product with entrepreneurial spirit that has the resources and backing of a much larger company. With [ORG], she was focused on APAC initially and then expanded to US Government, US Sales and project delivery. She had end-to-end oversight from lead generation to project management and project delivery. She also managed vendors (individual contributors that they outsourced to).
Based on your experience, what are the major use cases for AI data services? Please rank them from largest to smallest in terms of market size.
As she explains, there are two broad categories: 1. Language specific and 2. Language Agnostic. | 1. Language specific includes things such as NLP (data transcription services, etc.). These require more language expertise. 2. Language Agnostic includes computer vision projects i.e. autonomous vehicles, OC optical character recognition, etc. These can be high in revenue, larger in [ORG], but lower in profiability as everyone is competing for delivery based on cost and margins.
Can you to speak to who the main types of customers are for AI data services? What is the most relevant way of segmenting customers (e.g. by industry, by size, by other criteria), and why?
Yes; by industry and by size. For most, the largest clients are banks. For serious players, they will generally account for 70-80% of your overall revenue. For the rest, you have room for specialization (i.e. Government, etc.) The remainder of the large accounts are what they classify as enterprise accounts, but the spending is nowhere near the top 4.
Can you also speak to what buyers’ most important criteria are when deciding which provider(s) to purchase AI training data from? Does this vary by data type, buyer size and/or industry? Please briefly elaborate.
Price does matter, but it matters more to the smaller players that are tighter in budget (i.e. startups). For larger and more serious buyers, price is a secondary consideration to things such as reliability (quality), timelines and scalability. This is why despite the fact there are so many competitors out there, the older and more established companies are more dominant. They have built themselves in a way to remain scalable.
Can you speak to who the major providers of AI data services are and how they differentiate from one another? Please elaborate.
[ORG] is still the largest and most dominant but they have been experiencing some negative share action. Additionally, Lionsbridge (now called [ORG]). In computer vision, there is [ORG] and [ORG]. She has also seen [ORG] as a competitor.

[PERSON]: Hello and welcome to [ORG]’s Interview today entitled AI & Database Management – Sector Update & Market Outlook. [PERSON]: Could you please give an introduction to yourself, as well as your background and experience in the AI and cloud database software industry?

[PERSON]: I am a Director at [ORG]. I lead a team of solutions architects and some of the engineers. I’m responsible of dozens of application deployments, internal and external, and I lead the deployments from POC to production and ensure that they run as optimally as possible. I focus on a lot of different technologies. My background is heavily focused on data analytics, real-time analytics and artificial intelligence. I believe that artificial intelligence is integrated in a lot of our technologies today. In every different type of solution, you have some type of AI. Every company is becoming AI, so AI is much focused, at least in my solutions, in machine learning model deployment, low-code approach, but also language processing, computer vision, edge AI and so on, and also in the IoT space. I have much focus in terms of computer vision and maintenance of different types of centres, autonomous driving and using optical design to be able to support heavy AI workloads and so on. My expertise is not only on data analytics, but also in AI and in IoT, so IoT also includes metaverse and Dataverse and so on, and finally in data centres design and security.

Q: Could you highlight 2-3 key trends in how cloud database management vendors are leveraging AI for their database analytics solutions?

[PERSON]: If we look at the market today, it’s extremely important for database vendors to include AI in their models. There are two types of AI. There are the consumer-facing type of solution and there are also internal solutions that they provide capability with that. If we look at the consumer-facing solutions, what does it mean? Let’s say that you have a machine learning model that you want to deploy to the cloud and you have solutions such as [ORG] and [ORG] that allow you to do inference of machine learning models on their database. Now you can start running your machine learning models directly using a [ORG] or a [ORG] or even other major cloud providers’ solutions, such as [ORG] and [ORG] and so on. This is one piece of it, which is the consumer piece of it. The second piece of it is in terms of security and data loss prevention, and classification of the data and PII data and recommendations on running better queries in major database systems. A lot of AI is being incorporated into in terms of analysing the queries that users are writing into these database management solutions, and in terms of recommending better queries or better indexing or highlighting some of the PII data and specifications of the data automatically out of the box. This is how AI is being incorporated into also that product set.

Q: A [ORG] report stated that the AI software market will reach almost USD 134bn by 2025. Over the next five years, the market growth will accelerate from 14.4% CAGR in 2021 to 31.1% in 2025. Another report estimated a USD 209bn TAM by 2029. For such a large industry with so many use cases, how should we think about sizing the TAM, and do these estimates sound right to you?

[PERSON]: Yes, it may sound correct, but we need to be careful when we think about these numbers, because there is a lot of hype that is happening in a lot of technologies, and other technologies are inevitably going to grow, but there will be some technologies into this market that are not going to grow. Differentiating in terms of, the opportunities would be there, for sure, because of everything is turning towards digital and you’re going to need some kind of combination of security, AI and cloud management and data management solutions or all under one vendor. Offering all of these features is going to provide, may be too much for some vendors and may not be much of enough. The opportunity would be there, but this does not mean that everyone is going to be able to take advantage of these opportunities. Finally, a lot of companies set yearly budgets, but they cannot spend as much as they have expected to, because they cannot get to production as fast as possible. My fear is that a lot of these companies, they are going to see that there is a lot of money over there, but they’re not going to be ready to deliver the technology and where they will need to go.

Q: Are vendors doing a good job at gaining customer traction and expanding use cases? How much of a challenge is this proving to be and how are vendors approaching it for AI and database management software?

[PERSON]: It depends on the vendors and how they’re approaching all of this. I was reading a report yesterday about companies that didn’t make it in 2022 and I have seen a lot of these companies that were focused on retail, on autonomous driving, and there is a lot of hype into these technologies, but leadership, how you’re going to spend your money, how you’re going to continue growing your customer intake, a lot of start-ups also have failed around real estate, because ’21 was the year of real estate, ’22 we have seen a lot, with the interest rate going up, it was a little bit harder for these large data analytics companies. Being able to tackle all of these issues together is going to be important moving forward and ensuring that they can differentiate themselves and they are mature enough, by creating strong partnerships, and 
I insist by saying strong partnership. When you think about strong partnership, you can think about a partnership from United and [ORG] or Ford and [ORG] in driving and so on, so creating some strong partnerships to be able to sustain their product cost.

Q: Could you elaborate on which cloud database management vendors are doing the best job at expanding customer adoption?

[PERSON]: I would say [ORG] is extremely aggressive in terms of providing donations, providing partners that can deploy POCs, but we have seen some difficulties moving to productionalisation. Anything that is POC and demos are all heavily invested into [ORG] cloud, but moving to production, there was much more frustration. [ORG] has a lot of different products and has a lot of different features and has created some strong partnerships with some of the key vendors. I would say that there are some plus and minuses, but a lot of innovation is coming, at least from [ORG], from the AI point of view and cloud analytics, and from [ORG], it’s more an end-to-end solution. They view the cloud as an end-to-end solution and something 
that they really need to have some input into it. Having said all of this, when we think about cloud providers, also we think about the smaller ones such as [ORG], not as big, but they have a lot of networking and storage offerings and cost, and provide a lot of different solutions in terms of price for storage and zero-trust network security. We have seen them partner with a lot of different also customers to be able to expand their use cases very quickly.

Q: As the market has gotten tighter, we’ve seen some hyperscalers’ cloud usage dropping. How would you characterise the market’s growth for these solutions over the past year? Where were they hurting and which market areas continue to grow?

[PERSON]: I would say AI is definitely growing, but at the same time, people do not have the skills enough to be able to take advantage of the growth that is happening and the requested growth that is happening and demanded by the market. This is one piece of it. The second piece of it is in terms of, we’ve seen that the migration has already happened to most of the companies to the cloud, where most of them have at least 50% of their workloads in the cloud, or planning to move their workloads in the cloud. The issue is, can they get there enough and do they have the skills to get there enough? This is where we have seen a lot of challenges.

Q: You alluded to how [ORG] [automated machine learning] and various low-code solutions are changing the market. How will these technologies continue to be adopted and disrupt the market, and how are cloud companies reacting?

[PERSON]: [ORG] is a very cool feature, but it’s still not a complete feature. Where [ORG] does very well is it provides a machine learning model, but creating a machine learning model is only one piece of the pie in terms of supporting an end-to-end production machine learning deployment in production. Whenever you want to do an end-to-end machine learning solution in production, you need to be able to cleanse your data. This is where the pain is coming, generating your data, cleaning your data, making sure that your data is not imbalanced, making sure that it’s impartial, making sure that you’re implementing responsible AI, so human-centric types of solutions and so on. Then [ORG], where it does help you is just creating the model, then the deployment piece of it, there are a lot of yet features that are not complete and that need to be there, and it’s not all solved today by [ORG].

Q: How is low-code development changing the market, especially looking at the analytics solutions provided by various cloud vendors?

[PERSON]: It’s helping, but it’s not getting the product, it’s helping about knowing what the power of this solution can provide and by taking some of the different solutions directly to production, but it’s not yet replacing the end-to-end solution. There is still a lot of coding that needs to be taking place. We’re going towards the low-code approach, but I don’t think that a lot of enterprises are asking a lot of customisation and flexibility and low-code is not making it yet in terms of customisation and flexibility.

Q: Do you see the standard data warehouse vendors such as [ORG] or [ORG] leading the charge into more low-code solutions, or will it come from more data science-heavy machine learning lakehouse vendors such as [ORG] or [ORG]?

[PERSON]: I would say it should be coming from both. If it’s not coming from both, they wouldn’t be able to differentiate themselves moving forward into the cloud.

Q: How long do you think it will take before low-code solutions become more ubiquitous for cloud analytics solutions?

[PERSON]: [ORG] and [ORG] and [ORG] have been already providing a lot of the virtualisation platforms and a lot of the features that are extremely low-code, where you do not have to pick indexes and you do not have to pick partitions. Everything is done for you out of the box, so that they did already start the process. Yes, customers still need to do no-coding and all of this stuff, but they are working towards this. I feel that they are in a good place for success, those three companies.

[PERSON]: How long do you think it’ll take before adoption becomes more mainstream for these features?

[PERSON]: Some of them are already mainstream, such as partitioning, indexing, all of them are done in a low-code approach. Recommendation engines are all done in a low-code approach, allowing you to query your database and allowing you to share your data across vendors and marketplaces, and picking some of the marketplaces is all done in a low-code approach. Some of them are already in production, I would say.

Q: A [ORG] study projected that 60% of all data used in the development of AI will be synthetic rather than real by 2024. [ORG] launched a synthetic data platform in 2022 called [ORG]. Markets with traditionally inaccessible data such as healthcare are a clear opportunity to be able to use synthetic data. Can you comment on the market opportunity for synthetic data and how various vendors may be able to take advantage or be disrupted by this technology?

[PERSON]: Today, we have a lot of data, so we don’t have an issue about the data and the vast majority of the data, but guess what? All of the data is coming from one source of truth. You have a lot of data from one source of truth. You don’t have a lot of data from all of the different sources that you need to get to, unfortunately. If you tell me, “Do we have a lot of data?” I would tell you no, we don’t have a lot of data. We have a lot of not quality data, but we have a lot of quantity data, which does not translate well for quality data. This is why, when we test with autonomous driving our AI models, a lot of them fail, because the data is not quality data. This is why you’ve seen a lot of companies like [ORG] and other types of companies coming out and making sure that the data that we have is a rich data. That is one piece of it, is diversifying the data that we have, and the second piece of it is once you have the data, who’s going to label it? Who’s going to train it? How are you going to do all of this? If you have synthetic data, it’s going to be automatically labelled, so you reduce human error. It becomes much more humancentric, because you know what you’re missing and you know how you want to generate your additional data, and you can go with all of this data. Synthetic is going to be a huge market moving forward, and I see a lot of opportunities in this market. I think it’s going to be the most important market. One thing about synthetic data, we’re not just thinking about images, but we’re thinking about also database data, social types of data, and there are a lot of companies that focus on this kind of data, like mostly AI and other companies, so there are two pieces of it. Synthetic data is going to grow, there is no question about it, but also synthetic data is not coming from magic. Synthetic data gets created from existing data, so you need to create machine learning to create the data and it doesn’t come from thin air. Also we need to be careful that you have a lot of different types of use cases and you can diversify on getting the synthetic data. It’s not like you go to a company, let’s say [ORG], because you’ve given an 
example, and tell them, “I need to create synthetic data for my use case where I’m using energy, where I want to demand the forecasting of how much energy I’m going to need to spend for 2024.” They can’t generate data on this. They need to have machine learning, they need to train all of this, so it takes time. It’s not like a magic solution too, and I keep on insisting on magic, but really this is the take of it.

Q: How confident are you that synthetic AI solutions may become a part of product portfolios for cloud database analytics vendors such as [ORG] or [ORG]? Could these offerings become significant drivers of their revenues and for increasing consumption?

[PERSON]: Yes, and today, if you look at these vendors, they have already partnered in terms of marketplace with a lot of different types of companies that provide synthetic data, and you’re going to need, sooner or later, synthetic data. I would say it’s a little bit early for [ORG] and [ORG], for them to invest into it, but they are already creating partnerships around them.

Q: The [ORG] report I cited said that 60% of data being used for development of AI would be synthetic by 2024. How long might it take for synthetic data to become a more viable investment opportunity for cloud database management analytics vendors? If not 2024, what date might it be?

[PERSON]: I would say it’s already happening in some of the industries such as autonomous driving, and even in the retail industry, it’s happening. I would say in other industries, it has been slower. Health, it has been happening, but in other industries like CPG and other industries, they may have been slower. If it’s not today, it has to be literally tomorrow, literally the first day of 2023.

Q: Large language models such as NLP [natural language processing] and GPT [Generative Pre-trained Transformer]-3 using [ORG] have seen a lot of interest in 2022. One report states that nearly all of 2022’s largest AI financings were language start-ups such as [ORG] or [ORG]. What are the current or near-term use cases for this technology in the industry, and how would you expect it to grow and disrupt the market? One use case that comes to mind is chatrooms or chatbots. 

[PERSON]: Language understanding, questions and answers. These are all in addition to chatbots. What are chatbots? It allows you to understand the context, allows you to be able to answer and without alienating some of the end customers. Two years ago, when I would put myself in [ORG] of the chatbot, I would say, “No, it’s going to waste my time. It’s going to take so much,” so any vendors who would provide a chatbot solution, I would not be happy. Today, chatbots have done a lot of improvement, and today, it’s becoming much more context-driven. They allowed them to be much more performant compared to what it used to be. We need still to be careful. We do not need to share them as human. This is not where they are today, but if we are careful and we use them for specific use cases, we can create a lot of different 
types of multiple chatbot solutions and multiple models for each of the use cases and bring them all together to be able to deliver an end-to-end solution.

Q: [ORG]’s problems with its crowd workforce are well-publicised, with the company’s revenue growth also decelerating. What do you think happens to [ORG]’s margins given prices seem to be falling while contributors are still getting paid inappropriate sums?

[PERSON]: This applies particularly to the most commoditised part of [ORG]’s business, which is their 
annotation services. In the annotation services area, everybody involved knows the numbers. The crowd workforce knows what they should be getting paid. Many of them work for [ORG]’s competitors as well as for [ORG], and if one company is failing to offer the right amount of money, they’ll just stop working for that company and go and work for a competitor. They know what the prices are. The clients know what the prices should be for that, so they know what [ORG]’s costs are and what [ORG]’s competitors’ costs are, and they more or less dictate the price. [ORG] would have had strictly downward pressure on what they paid the workers until fairly recently, but in the last couple of years there’s been some pretty negative publicity, as you’ve said. That negative publicity has led to the biggest clients coming to [ORG] and saying, “We still want the cheapest service we can possibly get, but you also have to guarantee to us that you’re paying people a reasonable wage,” and, therefore, they’re being squeezed at both ends. They’re being told, “You must pay your workers properly and you must give us the smallest margin possible, sell to us at the smallest margin possible.”

Q: How do customers verify that contributors are getting paid well or paid within requirements? Is [ORG] handing over payslips or anything of that sort?

[PERSON]: No, I don’t think there’s that level of visibility, but you simply have to go to the Googles, the Amazons, the Apples, etc, you have to go to them and say, “Yes, we guarantee that we are meeting these things.” If they found that you weren’t, it would be very bad for your relationship. That’s about the level [ORG] is at.

Q: How can a company offset the type of margin squeeze that’s occurring at [ORG]?

[PERSON]: I think [ORG] has a strategy in place to try to transition to a more technology-driven offering across the board. That’s not new, it was on the radar for us from the early 2000s, that we needed to automate as much as possible, be a technology-forward company and, as you’ve no doubt observed, they’re making acquisitions which they believe will lead them to being a more technology-driven offering, and that will help them to operate at better margins, but make no mistake, their largest revenue sources remain the top group of technology companies and those technology companies are buying services offerings. They’re not really interested in buying the technology offering. [ORG] has to build an entirely new client base for that technology-forward offering to be meaningful.

Q: Who do you think are the customers of [ORG]’s technology offering?

[PERSON]: I think their play on this is that if you think about their core customers for a long time have been the top 10 technology companies, dominated by that top 10 technology companies. They’re making technology that other people use. [ORG]’s play is that they think they can make a better business, or a longer-term, more sustainable business, by targeting more like Fortune 500 companies, as opposed to a small group of 10, and that those companies are not developing the core technology, but they’re more tuning technology to their needs and that [ORG] will have a niche where they can provide them with more technology-driven support. 

[PERSON]: Is there a need for it? If you’re a Fortune 500 company, this is, or probably it feels like it’s going to be a very small component of your actual tech need requirements.

[PERSON]: If you look at the AI market, one of the big splits in the AI market is between technologies that work with unstructured data and technologies that work with structured data, so financial sector and things like that, you have very structured data, therefore, you don’t need external annotation to make that useful. If the data comes with useful metadata in the first place, then you don’t need to actually buy data or to pay for annotation of the data to make it useful, whereas in the unstructured space, which is the search, the social media, potentially healthcare markets and things like that, where it’s free text composed by humans, there you need annotation to interpret it and to add semantics and meaning to the data so that you can actually build the AI.

[PERSON]: I’m trying to think about it like where would this ability actually go? If you were a Fortune 500 company, such as [ORG], where would this go? Would this go in the tech department or would this be more in the marketing side?

[PERSON]: I think in the [ORG], they’re probably in that structured data space. That might be in the marketing area, but not so much in the tech area. The kinds of things that they’d be looking at, they’d be looking at synthetic data generation. Synthetic data generation has a pretty narrow audience. It’s most suited to people working in image data and things like that. You take seed data and then you build lookalikes to address different scenarios, if you like. Honestly, I’m saying this is about my opinion, my opinion is that this is a pretty tough market to crack, but I’m pretty sure that that’s what they’re attempting with the technology acquisitions they’ve done.

Q: What’s your sense of the progress [ORG]’s made in getting its tech offering to market?

[PERSON]: It’s probably been disappointing. One of the first technology-forward acquisitions that [ORG] made for was for [ORG]. I think that [ORG] thought there was going to be technology that would actually enhance the annotation process and it turned out that really the technology was only enhancing the onboarding process, which is still meaningful, but all it means is that it makes them a more efficient services company. In the case of [ORG], anecdotally I think [ORG], they expected again to be buying more technology than they got. I think they wound up with a workforce. They didn’t wind up with as much technology as they expected.

Q: How prevalent is contributors’ use of VPNs or other methods to increase their pay?
[PERSON]: It’s probably fairly prevalent. I’d have to dig into specific cases, because there are always people trying to cheat the system. Let’s say you’re doing annotation for Cantonese as spoken in Hong Kong, which uses the traditional script system and a Hong Kong accent, you may have people who are in a lower-cost market from China itself, using VPNs to say, “Yes, I’m in Hong Kong,” and cheating the system that way. It does often become apparent when you do proper quality control that they’re not what they claim to be.

[PERSON]: Is it 10% of the user base, or is it much more than that?

[PERSON]: It’s probably most prevalent in English. English is by far the biggest language for most of these technologies, since the US remains the biggest market for AI, and so you would have people who are speakers of Indian English, for example, or Filipino English, trying to work on US English projects and get paid at a US rate, which US rates are not the highest rates, but it is a very big market and they are much higher than Indian rates or Filipino rates. It’s fairly prevalent there and fairly prevalent, fairly difficult to pick up, and would have an impact on their quality and, therefore, their selling capability.

Q: How has [ORG] tried to rectify the situation of contributors falsifying their locations? Is it like playing whac-a-mole and once one thing comes down, another pops up?

[PERSON]: For some projects, you just can’t see it. It doesn’t affect the project cost, because if you’re doing a US English project, the only thing that’s happening is that you are overpaying somebody from India who is not really qualified to do the work in the US. It doesn’t change their cost. They were already expecting to pay the US rate, but they were expecting to pay it to US people. The real damage is if it affects the quality and the client rejects the data and sends it back for rework. It’s not actually at the coalface where the cost is affected.

[PERSON]: So it’s not necessarily between the company and the contributor, it’s more like the customer and the company, and then it’s got to go back and be done again.

[PERSON]: Yes, and reworking the same data without charging the customer any more is not an uncommon scenario and it’s a thing that really does affect profitability. It’s much better, if you get it right the first time, that’s really good for profitability. If you have to redo things at no extra pay then that’s pretty bad.

[PERSON]: Just on that then, in terms of that rework, how many times does something have to be reworked for it to become unprofitable?

[PERSON]: If you had to rework an entire dataset, it would be unprofitable. You would hope that that’s not the norm, that it might be a subset of the data or something like that, or that you could do no rework, and it does depend on the customer’s model. Some customers, they accept that what they want is the lowest possible price and they accept that there’s going to be a proportion of bad data in that lowest possible price, so in those cases they don’t send it back for rework, they just expect you to be cheap in the first place. Other customers have very, very high expectations. It’s not across the board that you’re affected by that, but it’s a real part of the picture.

[PERSON]: Everyone is aware there’s overlap between contributor bases across the market, but what is the competition for new contributors like? Is it relatively efficient?

[PERSON]: The turnover in crowds is pretty high, so if [ORG] says, “We’ve got a million people,” it’s probably not the same million people that they had two years ago. It’s likely that the majority of that has turned over during that time. Again, this is quite language- and region-dependent. In some regions, people are doing this for kicks. In other regions, people are seeing it as a career. You might get slightly better retention in some of those places. If it’s a language where there’s always work and it’s a region where this kind of crowd work is seen as something that’s actually a reasonable source of income, and if you contrast Scandinavian work, for example, Scandinavian work is likely to be students who are looking for a bit of extra pocket money, whereas some of the lower-cost economies, people are doing this because they need to live.

Q: What benefit does a platform gain by paying an above-market rate? Is anyone paying above local minimum wage?

[PERSON]: The expectation is usually that you do pay a little bit above minimum wage. If you shoot for
minimum wage, you’re likely to get poor quality and have problems with recruitment. Speed of recruitment is very important to profitability. For example, in some situations there are multiple suppliers working on the same project. Let’s say a [ORG] or an [ORG] has a lot of data that they need to be annotated, they might have [ORG], [ORG], [ORG], [ORG] all working on this and they might say, “We’ve got 10 million annotations required for the next week on this particular data subset.” They put that whole 10 million up. If you’ve got more people working then that pool of data is, essentially, available to all four players, so if one of those players has twice as many people on it as everybody else, they will get more of the business. It’s not like they’re saying, “We’re going to give two-and-a-half million to each of you, off you go.” They’ll actually put the 10 million files out there, or the 10 million tasks out there, and whoever does the most, fastest, gets the most business. Paying a little bit higher can mean that you have a bigger workforce, and if you’ve got the bigger workforce you get a bigger share of the pie in those settings. It’s not always like that, but that is definitely one of the ways that they work and that is one of the settings where paying slightly higher than everybody else would make sense.

[PERSON]: From your observation then, in terms of all these platforms, who is generally paying the most?

[PERSON]: That, I don’t know. I doubt that there is anybody that’s consistently paying the most, because all of them are shooting in the dark to some extent. Sorry, [PERSON], just another point in relation to that, sometimes these things require somebody that speaks language X or Y and that’s the only requirement, but there are other times when there are demographic requirements in order to make sure that you’re covering the perspectives of a whole community.
