[ORG], Former Algorithm Researcher
Name: [PERSON] ([PERSON])
Topic: Computer Vision Technology Development amid the AIGC Trends

ES: Hello and welcome to [ORG]’s Interview entitled computer vision technology 
development amid the AIGC trends. My name is [PERSON], [ORG]’s Sector Analyst, and I will be facilitating today’s Interview. We are very pleased to have [PERSON], who is a former Algorithm Researcher at [ORG], with us today. [PERSON], before we start today’s Interview, please state if you agree or disagree to the following statement: You understand the definition of material non-public information and agree not to disclose any such information, or any other information which is confidential, during this Interview.

[PERSON]: I agree.

Q: Could you briefly introduce your specialities and work experience related to AIGC (AI-generated content)?

[PERSON]: At first, I engaged in facial recognition technology research at [ORG]. Later, I worked on technologies for the understanding, editing and publishing of short videos or smartphone videos at [ORG] and [ORG]. Facial recognition technologies are often used to perform AIGC tasks. [ORG], for instance, used those technologies to enable features that allow users to change their gender or age, add sunglasses or masks and even swap the faces of people in videos. Those intelligent editing features all fall under the category of AIGC. Previously, I developed image editing features mainly based on GANs (generative adversarial networks). Last year, with the rise of [ORG], a text-to-image diffusion model, many players, including [ORG], rolled out the image or face editing features based on this model.
Short video publishing technologies may also involve AIGC features. [ORG] rolled out a popular feature last year, allowing users to turn their photos into cartoon-style images. From what I know, this feature was developed by its video publishing team based on the [ORG] model. I am quite familiar with short video publishing technologies. Besides, the understanding of short videos requires the processing of multiple modalities of data, including video data, audio data as well as text data such as titles and tags.I also have experience in developing technologies used to edit smartphone videos, such as erasing the background or removing a person who accidentally appeared in a video. Those intelligent editing features also belong to the category of AIGC.

Q: Could you explain in simple words the differences among large AIGC models across different modalities, including texts, images, audio and videos, in underlying technologies and other aspects?

[PERSON]: Large AIGC models can be divided into two main categories, namely those process modalities with obvious temporal characteristics, such as texts, audio and videos and those processing images, which are static and do not have temporal characteristics. [ORG] is a representative large model used to process texts with obvious temporal characteristics. Ever since [ORG] published a paper about the transformer in 2017, it has become common to process texts with temporal characteristics using transformers, which allow for autoregressive text generation, where the next word is predicted according to the previous words given.
Audio and videos can be generated in exactly the same manner as texts. For example, transformers can predict the next, say, 0.5 seconds of audio according to the previous five seconds of audio given to it. They can also predict the next frame according to the previous frames of videos. Every 0.5 seconds of audio, every frame of a video or every word of a text input is encoded into an embedding form in a space with a certain dimension, for example, 1,024.Transformers make predictions in the embedding space. They use decoders to convert an audio embedding predicted with the 1,024-dimension feature into a 0.5-second audio clip or a video embedding predicted with the 1,024-dimension feature into a frame. The decoders in transformers are used to generate predictive content in the form of embeddings and transform the embeddings into corresponding modalities, whether texts, audio or videos.
Images do not have temporal characteristics, so there is no such thing as predicting the next image, unlike the idea of predicting the next word. Previously, GANs were widely used for image generation, but at present, the [ORG] model is used more commonly. [ORG] is a physical diffusion model that can randomly add noise to images, eventually to the extent that the images are fully covered with noise. Then it can use trained algorithms or networks to remove the noise added and restore the images. Diffusion is the process of adding noise to normal and clear images.When [ORG] is used to generate images, it is the reverse process of diffusion. Textual prompts and prompts about image contours are introduced to remove the noise from random images covered with noise, so as to make the images clearer and eventually become a real image with content that meets users’ requirements. It is like clearing the fog away to see what lies behind. This is a process of iteration. Diffusion models should be able to restore images and have multimodal capabilities. Users certainly do not want to generate random images. They need to input reasonable texts describing the images that they want the models to generate, such as an image where there is a cartoon cat or an image where there is a person sitting at a table. Therefore, diffusion models must have multimodal capabilities to integrate texts and images and align the embedding feature space of texts with that of images to make sure the images they generate fit users’ descriptions. [ORG] and other diffusion models used to generate images should incorporate technologies for noise removal and have the ability to understand the text or other types of constraints. If they are given a sketch or an outline, they need to understand that the image they generate should share the same contours as the sketch or outline. At present, large AIGC models are generally divided into two categories, namely those generating texts, videos and audio as well as those generating images. Models in the latter category are typically diffusion models.

Q: What are the parameter sizes and training data volumes of large models used to generate texts, audio and videos with temporal characteristics and those used to generate images without temporal characteristics? Is there a big gap between them? What about the parameter sizes and training data volumes of large CV (computer vision) models?

[PERSON]: There is nothing wrong with your question, but before answering your question, I want to make it clear that there is an overlap between large CV models and large AIGC models, but they are not identical concepts. There are still many reports that confuse these concepts, so I think it is necessary to clear up the understanding first. I think the large CV models can be divided into three types. First, there are large perceptual models, which perform visual perception tasks such as classification, segmentation, labelling and detection. Their main application scenario is autonomous driving, where they enable autonomous vehicles to identify objects on the road, such as trees, pedestrians and vehicles. Now there are also large perceptual models based on the transformer technology. The Segment Anything Model (SAM) recently released by [ORG] is one example. It can cut out any object in any image, even though it cannot tell what the object is, for example, whether the object is a cup, a table or a bottle.
Second, there are large generative models, which correspond to the visual modality of AIGC. AIGC itself generates many modalities, including texts, images and videos. Images are mainly generated with large generative models, which represent a type of large CV models, particularly the [ORG] model. Then, transformers can insert some intermediate frames between the key frames or images generated by [ORG] to create a video. This is how images and videos are generated. Third, there are large multimodal models, which are also a type of large CV models. They can give titles to visuals or images and describe image content with texts. Those are multimodal visual tasks rather than tasks based on one single modality, for example, images. Large CV models can be divided into several categories. Those used in the AIGC field gained momentum at a relatively early point, but now the large models used in other fields are also evolving.Let us get back to your question. The overall parameter size of [ORG] is more than three billion, which is not very large. If you download a completely uncompressed model in the float32 format, its file size may be a little more than 10GB. Through methods such as compression, pruning and quantisation, the size of the model file may be reduced to only a little over 3GB, which can be processed by a regular GPU (graphics processing unit). The image generation process is quite complicated. [ORG] has several components. The first 
component is a text encoder, which enables the model to understand the texts input by users. The second component is an image encoder-decoder, which can turn images into small feature maps and then turn the feature maps back into images. Another important component is the U-Net architecture, which performs the key task, that is, to remove noise from noisy images and restore the images. U-Net accounts for less than one billion of the total three billion parameters of [ORG], occupying a little more than 1GB of storage space. Among all the components of [ORG], U-Net consumes the most computing power because it 
needs 40-50 repeated computations to remove noise and generate a clear and high-quality image. Text encoding, image encoding and image decoding each just require one single computation. U-Net needs 40-50 computations for noise removal. This requires a total computing power of about 1012 FLOPS (floating-point operations per second). An [ORG] [ID] GPU can iterate 20-40 computations per second, whereas U-Net can iterate 20-40 computations per second. It takes 40-50 computations to generate an image of good quality. Therefore, on average, [ORG] needs to run about two seconds on an [ORG] [ID] GPU to generate a high-quality or high-definition 512×512 image. [ORG] cannot fully utilise the parallel matrix computing power of [ORG] [ID] GPUs because it involves a relatively complex computational process. Its performance does not differ significantly whether it runs on high-end [ORG] [ID] GPUs or on ordinary [ORG] GeForce RTX 4090 GPUs, which are 
mainly for household use. Other models, for example, [ORG], may be unable to perform many tasks on ordinary household GPUs. Their performance on ordinary household GPUs may be 100 times worse than that on high-end GPUs. [ORG]’s performance may improve by only 3-4 times when it runs on [ORG] [ID] GPUs rather than [ORG] GeForce RTX 4090 GPUs because its computational process is complicated and fragmented.One of [ORG]’s main characteristics is that it can only be regarded as a medium-to-large-sized model when it is used to generate images because it can complete the image generation process on one single GPU instead of multiple GPUs. [ORG] can generate 512×512 images on a 16G [ORG] 
GeForce RTX 4090 GPU. If you want to generate 1,024×1,024 images with this model, you may need to use a GPU with higher capacity, maybe an 80G [ORG] [ID] GPU; if you just need 512×512 images, a 16G [ORG] GeForce RTX 4090 GPU would suffice. [ORG] is called a large model because its training process is long and requires multiple GPUs. From the standard version of [ORG] to the [ORG] version 1.5, the total training time is about 150,000 GPU hours. The GPU hour is the standard unit of training time. Training a model on an 
[ORG] [ID] GPU for an hour is one GPU hour of training. [ORG]’s total training time is 150,000 GPU hours, which means that the model has been trained for 150 hours or about 7 days if 1,000 GPUs are used. Various datasets have been used for the training of [ORG] and the largest one is Open Images, which has a total of nine million images. I am not sure if [ORG] has been trained with mixed data from multiple datasets or with cleaned data. The largest single dataset used to train it includes nine million images. I assume it has been trained with more than 10 million images.I can also talk a little about [ORG], a model that generates texts. The statistics published by its developer [ORG] shows that [ORG] has been trained with 45TB of text data, which may involve more than 10 trillion words. Some people say that one image is worth 1,000 words, but I do not think the figure is that high in many cases. I think a more reasonable estimate is that one image is worth 100-1,000 words. [ORG] has been trained with more than 10 trillion words. In other words, it has been trained 
with several ten billion images, which is very remarkable. Most other large CV models, including [ORG], have only been trained with several million or at most several ten million images. The gap between [ORG] and [ORG] in training data volume is quite big.
There is a gap between them in training time as well. [ORG] has not revealed details about [ORG]’s raining process, so it is quite challenging for other market participants to reproduce the process. They may train some small models and magnify the results to estimate the training time of [ORG]. It is estimated that the training time of [ORG] is around 3-5 million GPU hours, whereas that of [ORG] is about 150,000 GPU hours. The former is roughly 10 times the latter. The gap between [ORG] and [ORG] in training time is smaller than the gap between them in training data volume. If a company wants to train a large model like [ORG], it probably needs to use 10,000 GPUs and spend 2-4 weeks for training, if there are not any failures or restarts during the training process. The actual training time is typically longer than expected because there will inevitably be failures and restarts, especially when many GPUs are used. I can also cite the training statistics of some other large CV models for your reference. [ORG]’s SAM, for instance, has been trained on only 256 GPUs for 68 hours or less than three days. Its total training time is 18,000 GPU hours, which is much shorter than that of [ORG] or [ORG]. Generally speaking, large models can be divided into those with a training time of over 10,000 GPU hours, those with a training time of over 100,000 GPU hours and those with a training time of over one million GPU hours. [ORG], which generates texts, is the only one that has reached a training time of over one million GPU hours. [ORG] has a training time of about 100,000 GPU hours. Other large models used to perform perceptual tasks, such as segmentation and text-image multimodal processing, may only have a training time of several ten thousand GPU hours.

Q: It seems that model training costs are determined by GPU hours. Could you estimate the model training cost per GPU hour?

[PERSON]: The model training cost per GPU hour is quite easy to estimate because the prices charged by domestic and foreign public cloud service providers for renting out GPUs are transparent. For example, [ORG]’s [ORG] ([ORG]) charges an average price of USD 0.99 or about USD 1 for renting out an [ORG] [ID] GPU for one hour. In this sense, if companies rent [ORG]’s [ORG] [ID] GPUs for model training, their model training cost per GPU hour is USD 1. However, there are many other issues to consider, such as the costs of labour and infrastructure construction. If a company can procure GPUs and build server rooms by itself, its model training cost per GPU hour can be decreased below USD 1, but in this case, it needs to take care of daily operations and maintenance (O&M), disaster recovery and everything on its own. If it rents the GPUs of public cloud service providers, the service providers can help it handle some O&M issues.

Q: With the popularity of [ORG], the demand for large AI models has surged and the cost of model training has increased. Will the reduction in model training costs further boost the development of the large AI model market?

[PERSON]: Yes. I think the overall computational cost and complexity of [ORG] may gradually decrease in the future because the model has already been trained with a whopping 45TB of text data, which involves roughly 10 trillion words and covers almost all high-quality English corpuses available, including websites or webpages as well as publicly published books and papers that can be accessed on the internet. Therefore, there is not much room for its training data volume to increase further. I think the problem with it is how to obtain high-quality data with better cost-effectiveness. If there is no more room for data growth and improvement, the increases in the size of the model cannot create much value or yield much of a return.

Q: You mentioned that the model training cost per GPU hour is about USD 1 at present. How much has the cost changed over the past few years? With the improvement of infrastructure and computing power, how will the cost change in the future? Will we see something similar to the Moore’s Law in the market?

[PERSON]: With the improvement of GPUs’ computing power, the model training cost is bound to decrease. The computing power of [ORG] [ID] is 10 times that of [ORG] [ID], but the cost of [ORG] [ID] is not this much higher than that of [ORG] [ID]. This leads to increased computing power per unit cost. Companies tend to train larger models on GPUs with higher computing power for better results. They will not reduce the number of GPUs used after the computing power of GPUs is increased because the whole point of developing and training large models is to push it to the limit. I think companies that are developing large models all hope to train their models on the most and best GPUs they can afford to use to get the best results. If companies can use 1,000 GPUs to train their models at present, few of them will reduce the number of 
GPUs used from 1,000 to 100 after the GPUs’ computing power is improved. Most of them will continue to use 1,000 improved GPUs or improved GPUs with a total cost comparable to that of the original GPUs they used to train their models for better performance. Some companies may wait until other companies’ large models are trained on 1,000 GPUs and are made open-source so that they just need to fine-tune the models for specific applications. It is not cost-effective for them to train a large model with 100 GPUs on their own. I think Moore’s Law will apply to this market at some point. Notably, however, [ORG] is currently the dominant supplier of GPUs, and this is unlikely to change in the short term. After all, [ORG] can provide not only GPUs with advanced process nodes but also a complete range of supporting software suites and tools that require a huge amount of code and workload, including CUDA. Other suppliers may be able to 
procure advanced 5nm or 3nm wafers to produce multi-core GPUs, but still, they will find it hard to sell those GPUs if they cannot provide support tools. Therefore, the GPU market is dominated by [ORG] at present.For the time being, it seems that [ORG] is the closest to breaking [ORG]’s dominance. [ORG] does not sell its TPUs (tensor processing units) to other companies, but it has developed some TPU-based commercial services, which have already been used by some companies. It is said that [ORG] or [ORG] uses [ORG]’s TPU service to support its drawing features. Now [ORG] maintains high profit margins on its GPUs and the profits it makes may be invested back in R&D. In the future, facing competitors such as [ORG], [ORG] is bound to lower the prices of its GPUs, but considering increases in the model size, the model training cost per GPU hour may not necessarily decrease.

Q: The parameters that [ORG] uses were data updated to 2021 or 2022. Is this because of the limitations of training costs and time? Or are there any other reasons? How much will the delay between the data used to train the model and the latest data available be shortened in the short term?

[PERSON]: Just released this year, GPT-4 (Generative Pre-trained Transformer 4) is the latest version of GPT. No one knows all the details about it yet because [ORG] has not made it open-source. Some people estimated that the parameter size of GPT-4 is 100 times that of GPT-3, but [ORG] said that the difference is not that large. Judging from the response time and memory size, I think GPT-4 certainly has more parameters than GPT-3, but the gap is not as large as 100 times. I have used GPT-4 myself. I think its response time is slower than that of the previous version mainly because its model size is bigger, but not because [ORG] has not allocated enough resources for its development.Besides, GPT-4 supports an input text length of up to 15,000 words, an increase of about 100% from the previous 7,000-word limit. This results in an exponential increase in computational complexity. Of course, there are methods to change the increase from an exponential one to a linear one, but still, the 100% increase in the input text length will cause significant increases in computational complexity as well as training and inference costs. The model must be trained with long texts if it is expected to process long texts GPT-4 can process up to 15,000 words at a time, which means that users can input the text of a whole webpage or a whole paper for GPT-4 to handle. This is very convenient. I think [ORG] decided to double the input text length instead of increasing the parameter size by, say, 2-3 times because it figured that the former improvement could bring better user experience.

Q: You mentioned earlier that [ORG]’s SAM can recognise different objects. Do you think models with automatic object recognition features will replace the services of Chinese data providers such as [ORG] or squeeze the space for those data providers to survive?

[PERSON]: I do not think the services of data providers have high barriers. Costs and regulatory compliance are the two most important factors for data providers to consider. First, data providers should make sure that the ways they source data are in line with regulations and do not violate any privacy rights. For example, if they want to get access to a platform’s data, they must obtain the platform’s authorisation. Second, data providers should control labour costs incurred in data collection. There are many data collection companies in China because the cost of labour in China is lower than that in western countries and Chinese people are generally less sensitive to privacy issues than western people. Legally speaking, it is less risky to acquire data from social media platforms or public online channels in China than it is in the US. Data collection companies or data providers can survive quite easily in China, so they are more 
replaceable than model developers. Models can develop labelling capabilities. With the popularity of [ORG], many other models start to use [ORG]’s answers as standard answers to users’ questions, which makes it unnecessary for humans to label the standard answers manually. [ORG] may be able to get the correct answers in 90% of cases and sometimes it can even answer questions better than humans do. Models are self-iterative and can use their own labels as supervisory signals. If answers are ready, people only need to clean and score them. They are just required to screen answers to eliminate the low-quality ones and leave the good ones. Similarly, after images are processed via SAM, people only need to check whether the segmented elements are right or not rather than segmenting elements one by one by themselves. The requirements and costs of human labour are different. Even middle-aged and aged people that have received primary education can segment elements on images, while the requirements are higher for people to conduct follow-up editing. Therefore, as the model 
capabilities improve, the possibility will be higher for them to substitute for data providers.

Those companies will have adequate technical reserves of text and large multimodal models Regarding technical capability, I think [ORG], [ORG] and [ORG], which has been renamed as [ORG], are in the first echelon. No Chinese companies are in the first echelon regarding the paper numbers and capabilities to make innovative achievements.
Nonetheless, some Chinese companies have tapped into this industry early, and they also have strong capabilities. Although [ORG] lags behind some foreign companies in basic architectures, processes and fine-tuning, it has still been ahead of the game considering that it released large models this year. in this field. I don’t know whether [ORG] has accumulated related technology or not. To my knowledge, it still uses one billion or ten billion parameters to train language models. Even if it didn’t have such a plan initially, I believe [ORG] would try such a large parameter volume because such a giant company will never let itself fall behind. Regarding technology accumulation for large models, [ORG] outperforms [ORG], and [ORG] has a more profound accumulation than [ORG]. [ORG] is another noteworthy player in addition to those large companies. It previously highlighted product launching but did not focus on models’ transfer learning. Nonetheless, [ORG] excels in computing power and facilities. If it wants, [ORG] can tap into this field quickly. However, [ORG] is business-driven, so it will not spend so much money on research work before seeing actual application scenarios of [ORG]. Among start-ups, two companies stand out. The first is [ORG]. It has broken through in making large models in the CV field and rolled out question-and-answer systems in the NLP (natural language processing) field. [ORG] has colaunched ChatGLM with [ORG], and the model’s parameter volume has also reached a hundred billion. The company is among the early Chinese movers that offer open-source resources to teach others to train ultra-large NLP models. [ORG] has certain advantages in pure research, and [ORG] also has facilities and technology to train large models, which makes the company stand out from start-ups.

[PERSON]: I did not mention Zhihu and its likes because I don’t see that they have technology accumulation. Q: The industry is still in an early stage, where large companies need the following core resources and capabilities: reserves of graphics cards and training cards with high computing power and high performance, algorithmic technology capability, long-term accumulation and understanding of this field. What do you think of the industry’s short, medium and long-term development regarding the three core resources and capabilities? [PERSON]: In addition to the three core resources and capabilities you have listed, data accumulation is also essential. There are barriers to accumulating data. Open-source data is easy to obtain, but the costs will be remarkably high to acquire data for actual business scenarios, such as ultra-large text and image annotation information. Moreover, the time issue is also significant because such information will not be ready after 1-2 weeks of preparation. Instead, such data requires constant iterations. When companies are unsatisfied with the annotation, they need to find someone to re-annotate it, and the process is time-consuming. Therefore, data accumulation also determines the gaps among companies. Speaking of computing power, [ORG] has huge GPU reserves for business development. I’m not quite sure about it because large companies will not directly reveal how many GPUs they have. As far as I’m 
concerned, [ORG]’s GPU reserves are more extensive than its peers. Hence, it has a significant advantage in applying those GPUs in model training. What matters is switching the reserves mainly for client use to internal R&D. For example, when [ORG] provides services to [ORG], it has temporarily transferred a lot of computing power from its internal cloud platforms to the project for [ORG]. If a cloud service provider’s new product needs a lot of computing power, it can also transfer the computing power of high-end GPUs from other projects to internal model 
training for a certain period, say, a month. This is an advantage of cloud service providers, and their graphics cards will not idle, but for either external clients or internal R&D. Among the aforesaid companies, I know [ORG] has purchased many GPUs. If it wants, the company can train models on a large [ORG]. Notably, it has become difficult for companies to acquire GPUs, and it will be harder and harder to get GPUs in the future for two reasons. Nonetheless, no one knows how the international situation China faces will change. It is possible that even though foreign companies have applied [ORG] [ID] or more advanced graphics cards on a large 
[ORG], Chinese companies still cannot access or use them as their foreign peers do

Q: Despite the bottleneck in computing power, I believe all large companies will have computing power reserves in the long term. Their core capabilities also rest on algorithms for fine-tuning and data acquisition. How do you compare Chinese companies in those aspects?

[PERSON]: Companies whose business is related to large models’ application scenarios will have significant edges in data acquisition. For example, in text chatbots, [ORG] has a natural advantage because it can crawl internet corpora constantly through its search engine. To improve the search results, [ORG] has a large annotation volume for text corpora.
[ORG] operates [ORG]. It doesn’t acquire data directly from [ORG] users’ chat logs but usually conducts text annotation in various corpora for product development. Even if [ORG] doesn’t train large models, it still has advantages in data accumulation because its main business is about that. In comparison, [ORG] can only get data from open-source corpora, which is enough for research. However, with that data, [ORG]’s products may be inferior to those based on self-annotated corpora. [ORG]’s edge rests on method exploration, but it lags behind others in actual business. [ORG], [ORG] and [ORG] have strong NLP capabilities. [ORG] has a lot of vertical corpora of commodities because its e-commerce platforms have high volumes of text data from its various commodity descriptions. In the vertical chat segment, those companies certainly have their advantages. In terms of AGI (artificial general intelligence), [ORG] stands out with its text corpora. In images, [ORG] and [ORG] have remarkable edges because they have various products, such as short video and livestreaming platforms. Those scenarios can provide a lot of visual information. When it comes to image 
generation and CV model development in large scenarios such as game design and art design, companies with games and graphics capabilities will have superiorities. As those companies have started developing games, they have data resources in art design. I don’t think there will be barriers to top talent for model training in the short term because staff turnover at internet companies is very high. Model training just needs a small number of top talent who know about the theory of large models. The training and architecture specialists can work with many 
O&M and hardware installation people to fulfil system tasks and deal with multi-system connections. When those top talent join new companies, they can quickly bring their know-how there. Hence, companies only need to acquire 1-2 experienced people and focus on assembling the primary team. It is not hard for the whole team to train models. However, companies are competing fiercely in acquiring top talent, and probably those offering high pay are relatively attractive to talent in this field.

Q: Could you introduce the development status quo of GPT-4? What do you think of GPT-4’s 
development in the short and middle term? What are the gaps in various aspects for Chinese companies to develop GPT-4? What is your view on such gaps and the speed for Chinese companies to catch up with their foreign counterparts?

[PERSON]: GPT-4 and [ORG] have amazed all people in the AI field, from those focusing on CV to those working with NLP. Their effects have even impressed the three giant companies engaged in artificial intelligence. When algorithms are developed to a certain extent, what they can realise will be beyond people’s expectations. I believe [ORG] and GPT-4 have already demonstrated surprising capabilities, even though some test results may not be perfect. For instance, when people search academic papers on [ORG] and GPT-4, the models may give them some papers that don’t exist. Nonetheless, the models have demonstrated a stable personality from their overall inference capabilities, mastering of general knowledge and logic in answering questions. Based on GPT-4, [ORG] and new Bing both show consistent speaking logic and personality. It has surprised us because those effects are beyond the original design but are achieved by fine-tuning the data. In terms of GPT-4’s development in the short to medium term, I think its performance has reached the basic standards required for it to be regarded as AGI. How GPT can be used by more people at the lowest costs hinges on related application scenarios, and developers should think about in which scenarios else people would need such an assistant besides search engines. For example, [ORG] has integrated GPT4 into its Office software to help people handle documents and write codes. What will the significance be for Chinese companies to reproduce the generic model of [ORG]? Undoubtedly, they do it to catch up with their foreign peers in technology accumulation. However, many Chinese followers are not clear about the specific application scenarios. If they don’t have existing products for integration in a way similar to what [ORG] does on [ORG] Office, Chinese developers should think about how their GPT-4 models can be applied apart from search engines. If they also develop question-and-answer systems, users will compare such question-and-answer systems with [ORG] and pay to use only one. Then, how can Chinese developers open a new way to success? [ORG] has developed many plugins based on [ORG], and users can book airline tickets and hotels through those plugins. Chinese developers should also think about the specific scenarios for GPT plugins while following [ORG]’s paces in technology progress. How do Chinese and foreign followers lag behind [ORG] in developing GPT? Giant foreign companies, such as [ORG] and [ORG], have been developing GPT, but they are still behind [ORG]. Still, I estimate their lag is less than a year. When there was still a half year or more for those companies to figure out how to realise designated effects through fine-tuning, [ORG] emerged. This was beyond those foreign giants’ expectations and made them rush their work. However, their current work results 
are not very good. This also involves many research directions, not just a matter of working hard for a few months to get similar results. Even so, those foreign companies can follow [ORG]’s reinforcement learning approach on [ORG] and may catch up with [ORG] and achieve effective results within a few more months.The gap between Chinese companies and [ORG] is more significant. Overall, Chinese companies are still product-driven and generally want to achieve instant results on products in the short term. GPT-related large language models had been popular for many years before reaching remarkable progress in around 
2021-22. Back then, some Chinese companies engaged in NLP already followed the development route of GPT, but they didn’t have rich experience. There are few open-source reinforcement learning codes, so the overall training and adjustment difficulties are significant. It is difficult to train a large model, and it will be harder for most Chinese companies to reproduce GPT models if they have not conducted reinforcement learning research and design. Therefore, I estimate the lag between Chinese companies and [ORG] in this field is over a year or even two years. Nonetheless, with emerging open-source communities, many developers have started studying reinforcement learning. If they can work together, I believe it will not take such a long time for Chinese companies to catch up with [ORG]. Suppose companies are incapable of training models with a thousand or even ten thousand GPUs. In that case, I reckon it will take more than a year and a half for their models to reach GPT-4’s level because those companies need to have the related facilities prepared and conduct fine-tuning. Again, it takes 
around a year for large companies with certain technology accumulation to catch up with [ORG]’s GPT-4.

Q: If we classify large models by application scenarios, how far away do you think those large models are from AGI, given the various restrictions we discussed? In which aspects are there still room for improvement?

[PERSON]: Text-based models are already close to AGI’s primary standards, while the distance between vision-based models and AGI is still far. There are many challenges involved, such as low-quality image data. Cleaning images is much more complex than cleaning text. For example, it is easy to check and screen duplicates in text, but it is much harder to determine whether two images are similar. With either automatic or manual measures, coping image data, say, recognising low-quality images, is more effortconsuming than dealing with text data.
Image files are large and require huge storage. The storage required by an image is much bigger than that by hundreds of words. Hence, to become qualified to be regarded as AGI, the resolution ratio for image storage, reading and caching should be accordingly high. For more authentic and better processing effects, the resolution ratio should not be as low as 24×24 but be similar to that of mobile phone photos. This is a great challenge. Hence, given the development status quo of CV models, I don’t think they can reach that level. We need to wait for at least two years to see how it has developed before making further estimates. A few days ago, an article about SAM gained popularity. The article did not introduce any notable innovative points but demonstrated the correct process of how SAM can be used to clean a large amount of data efficiently. It has applied large CV models in scenarios for segmentation and designed a reasonable task. In that task, all objects in the image are annotated, and the annotation process is automatic. While following this correct approach, there are still many data-related problems that need to be addressed. The most typical scenario for CV models to reach AGI’s level as NLP models do is being applied to robots. In all scenarios, such as home, road, outdoors and restaurants, the robots, equipped with CV models, can understand what they have seen and know what they should do accordingly. However, it is still far from seeing such high-level CV capabilities on robots. Despite the hope brought by SAM and NLP for text, I 
think the distance between CV and NLP for AGI is still far, and it still takes a long time for CV models to develop before reaching the desired level.

Q: Does the main bottleneck mainly rest on storage and computing power?

[PERSON]: The primary bottlenecks are about computing power and data. Annotating a large amount of data requires a lot of money and effort, and it is difficult for companies to get high-quality image data with trillions of parameters. Even if a company has acquired such data, annotating it through human labour is still effort-consuming.

Q: [ORG] provides AI video editing software and apps for the film-television industry. It has 
participated in the co-development of the initial version of [ORG]. Will such companies focus on the CV segment in the short term? What other development directions are noteworthy?

[PERSON]: [ORG] has many applications in various fields involving painting and image generation, such as visual AIGC, film and video editing, game design and interior decoration. However, it is hard to realise fully intelligent visual generation. In some application scenarios, [ORG] can even replace graphics engines. For example, in film and video editing, [ORG] can be used to create special effects, such as adding a ball of fire on some parts. Additionally, [ORG] can also be applied to scene composition in green screen shooting. Some CG (computer graphics) special effects for games and movies are created with images, and [ORG] is an ideal substitution for graphics engines in making such CG special effects for games and movies. Nonetheless, tools for generating video special effects don’t show remarkable commercialisation value compared with tools for generating image special effects. At present, there is no widely accepted general paradigm that determines the continuity, stability or dynamics of image frames and shots and how to describe videos and camera movement. Therefore, video generation tools cannot be commercialised immediately. Even so, this field has shown a clear potential for the future. [ORG] has already shown commercialisation promise in many illustration and game companies. Those companies have found that simple images drawn with [ORG] and [ORG] can still meet their requirements, so they have enlarged their applications of AI tools and recruited fewer junior designers. For housing decoration, users only need to provide floor plans or images of the house’s original conditions. Then, the tools will generate visual effect pictures of decorated housing that suit different styles and tastes. Those companies also have another advantage in that they can collect data for training from their previous design cases, thus avoiding risks such as the sources of data for training. They also have application scenarios. However, it is difficult for those companies to develop algorithms that can be quite suitable for every vertical field. Instead, they are more inclined to develop workable tools. To be more exact, those companies will provide users with tool and service packages, including basic foundation models and user interface and interaction tools, according to users’ images and specific requirements, for instance, sketches or floor plans for housing decoration. They will not directly offer trained models to clients. The product models are different in this regard.

Q: What are the promising directions for market development in the industry? Which companies and vertical segments are you optimistic about in the short, medium and long term?

[PERSON]: I’m mainly engaged in the CV field, and I think video editing in this field will be a promising direction. Besides, companies devoted to developing metaverse have created reliable AR (augmented reality) and VR (virtual reality) scenarios, especially the former. Generating models to transform reality objects into virtual scenarios, say, transforming real houses into game scenarios, is also a kind of exploration in the AR and VR segments. The application scenario for generative models also includes changing all objects in a room into cartoon images with a futuristic style for games or others. Another application scenario for generative models is related to planning and design.In addition, I think perceptron models will first be applied in the autonomous driving field, but many challenges need to be overcome. A significant challenge is that the FLOPS of vehicle-mounted graphics cards are not as high as the FLOPS of commercial graphics cards. With limited computing power, it is hard to make the performance close to that of large models. There are two ways to cope with the challenges. The first approach is to reduce the computing power required for large models. Second, having noticed the large scenario, vehicle-applied [PERSON] manufacturers are striving to increase the computing capacity of their products. Once the computing capacity of vehicle-applied chips reaches a certain level, large models can perform effectively. These are the promising directions that I've Identified.

ES: As time was limited, we will end the Interview. Thanks again to [PERSON] for their input today. Thank you again for joining [ORG]’s Interview. This now concludes our meeting. Goodbye.


[ORG], Former Head of Data Identification
Name: [PERSON] ([PERSON])
Topic: [ORG] – AI Training Data Service Providers’ Competencies

ES: Hello and welcome to [ORG]’s Interview entitled [ORG] – AI training data service providers’ competencies. My name is [PERSON], [ORG]’s Sector Analyst, and I will be facilitating today’s Interview. We are very pleased to have Mr Jiang, who is a former Head of Data Identification at [ORG], with us today.Mr Jiang, before we start today’s Interview, please state if you agree or disagree to the following statement: You understand the definition of material non-public information and agree not to disclose any such information, or any other information which is confidential, during this Interview.

[PERSON]: I agree.

Q: Could you briefly introduce your work experience and specialities in the data labelling industry?

[PERSON]: I entered the data labelling industry in 2018 and started as a project manager. At [ORG] Technology ([ORG]), I was mainly responsible for the operations of the data recognition business. [ORG] specialises in the automatic speech recognition (ASR) business and also provides image annotation services. It has business in all types of data labelling services with a certain influence on the whole market.

Q: What are the main types of data required for AI training? How to evaluate the market value of different data types in terms of potential and actual market size?

[PERSON]: Based on the actual application scenarios, there are three types of data, namely speech data, text data and image data. The main applications involving speech data are text transcription and speech generation. Specifically, speech data-based services are required when writing meeting minutes. The main purpose is to transcribe the speech into texts. Image data mainly covers applications with the demand for target recognition. Bounding box, keypoint detection and segmentation are the major image annotation methods, all of which aim to recognise the targets. The video surveillance system of City Eyeused to be an application with a large demand for image annotation. Nonetheless, the autonomous driving sector has been the main growth driver for the data labelling industry over the past few years. The research direction of text data applications has always been unclear because it involves semantic 
generalisation. The main purpose is to clarify the linguistic structure of the text and determine the subject, predicate and object, and which constituents can be replaced and which cannot. However, with the launch of [ORG], there has been a great change in the market involving text data services. Previously, the major focus was on data labelling, but now the focus has shifted to text data collection. [ORG] requires more raw data for training and manual supervision and optimisation. It is different from traditional data labelling tools, and its popularity has reinvigorated the market involving text data services. Currently, the ASR market seems to be saturated and gradually declining. This is due to the high accuracy rate of ASR tools and the absence of new application scenarios. The image annotation market has been growing steadily thanks to the popularity of autonomous driving. The text data service market used to be sluggish, but after the launch of [ORG], it has seen explosive growth this year. Nevertheless, the actual order volume remains small, despite the large number of consultations. It is expected that the data labelling industry will have a good performance in H2 2023. These are the main data types and my evaluation of their corresponding market value.

Q: According to your observation, what are the respective revenue proportions of these data application markets?

[PERSON]: The ASR contributes 10-20% of the revenue, the text data 10-20% and the image annotation around 70%.

Q: You mentioned that the growth of the ASR market has slowed down. Do you think the continuous improvement and technology iteration in the text recognition market will further boost the ASR market? Or are there no direct relations between the two markets?

[PERSON]: The effect won’t be significant based on the data labelling market because there are already many mature ASR models with good performance. If the language to be processed is Mandarin, these models can achieve high accuracy rates, sometimes surpassing those of human operators. ASR is more required for some vertical scenarios such as games, where there are many technical terms that cannot be transcribed by models. Some other scenarios involving obscure dialects also need ASR, while for common dialects and minority languages, the ASR tools have been well-developed. Currently, there are fewer large-[ORG] speech data collection and labelling orders. The orders are mainly for the optimising of certain vertical scenarios and have a value of less than RMB 500,000. In brief, there will still be ASR orders, but the form has completely changed. Previously, only basic models were required and the order volume was large. In comparison, the order volume is small currently and the orders mainly have a demand for vertical application optimisations.

Q: What is your estimate of the growth rate of the autonomous driving and image data sectors in the data labelling market?

[PERSON]: The autonomous driving sector has been growing at an annual rate of 20-30% since 2020. This sector has a market size of about RMB 1.5bn-2bn in China, probably more than RMB 3bn.
This year, at least three auto manufacturers have started their own data labelling business and stopped recruiting suppliers. [ORG] has launched its own data labelling platform and claims that it can realise automatic labelling and no longer outsource the data labelling business to companies such as [ORG]. Besides, [ORG] and [ORG] have also launched their own data labelling platforms and claim that they have automatic labelling capabilities. Although the order volume of the autonomous driving sector seems to be increasing, the business [ORG] of data labelling companies doesn’t seem to have changed much compared with last year. This is because they have to compete with their clients, instead of merely their peers in the industry.

Q: How will the cost change if the clients develop their own data labelling platform tools and have no procurement? The development of such tools involves large investments in the initial stage and talent cultivation requires a certain period. How long do you think it will be for these clients to gain cost-effectiveness?

[PERSON]: Chinese companies basically use multimodal data in images, point clouds and high-precision maps, so the data to be labelled comes from various sources. Especially nowadays, the amount of data is getting larger. The data has to be labelled every time it is collected. Sometimes, the cost can reach tens of millions or even hundreds of millions of RMB, which accounts for a large proportion of clients’ annual expenditures. In this case, they would rather develop independent data labelling capabilities rather than pay such a huge amount of money for outsourcing companies that they don’t completely trust for this business. There are several advantages for the clients to develop independent data labelling capabilities. First, they are clear about their data and algorithm types, so they can develop many customised functions according to their own needs and the labelling rules of their platforms, which can greatly improve efficiency. It’s impossible for outsourced companies to do this because they tend to develop universal functions rather than customised ones. This is the first advantage.
The investment in the first year will be large. Nonetheless, these clients can develop their own capabilities. Similar to many large companies, they are reluctant to be restricted by any link in the supply chain. Instead, they prefer to have control over the whole supply chain. Data is the most important component of the supply chain for all AI products, and clients don’t want all their data sources to be restricted by suppliers. [ORG] has established bases for data labelling. It takes about 3-5 years to recover the cost ranging from procedures including establishing bases, training personnel and stabilising the output, despite the low cost-effectiveness in the initial investment stage.

Q: Do companies focusing on autonomous driving only apply the independently developed data labelling tools or platforms? Or do they still procure some tools for common scenarios?

[PERSON]: They apply data labelling tools and platforms both independently developed and procured. [ORG] is an exception. All of its personnel for the data business are recruited. It has established its own data labelling platforms and cultivated relevant talent. This is the business model of [ORG]. In comparison, [ORG] is only responsible for the development of the platform and automatic labelling technology. [ORG], another supplier, provides data labelling personnel and sites and manages personnel for it. They are in a cooperative relationship. Different companies have different positioning. For example, [ORG] positions itself as a technology-based company and it prefers to focus on scientific research rather than company management.

Q: Some small and medium-sized human resource outsourcing groups have also entered the data labelling industry. Will the clients outsource single projects to such small teams while applying their own tools or platforms?

[PERSON]: Yes. The current market landscape is completely different from 3-4 years ago and there’s only a limited market size for small and medium-sized groups. In the past, there were only small and mediumsized suppliers and 10-20 employees could form a group to work in a small office. The number of such small suppliers is decreasing. The condition you mentioned still exists but the small suppliers are no longer mainstream in the industry. I think small and medium-sized suppliers have several ways to survive in the market. First, they can provide low-cost labour resources for auto manufacturers or data labelling companies such as [ORG]. That is to say, they can establish good strategic cooperative relationships with 1-2 clients with the advantages in labour. This is the first way for them to survive in the market. Second, many companies such as [ORG] and [ORG] have data labelling bases in China. [ORG] has established the Artificial Intelligence Data Annotation Centre in Shanxi. Some projects will have public recruitment on these bases. Some small suppliers will dispatch their employees to the bases which provide basic office facilities for them, but they have to be responsible for management and operations independently. The advantage of doing so is that they can enjoy stable project sources from these bases that have priority in implementing some projects. There’s a third case. Some suppliers may have gained excellent management capabilities and cultivated 
outstanding personnel after several years of development. They may gradually expand to have hundreds of employees and are competent enough to negotiate with clients for large orders. Such suppliers with advantages in labour cannot be considered small ones. Some companies may wonder why [ORG] can develop data labelling platforms independently but they cannot. This is because [ORG] is profitable and is willing to make investments in platform R&D. Nonetheless, such cases are few. Generally, Beijingbased companies with data labelling platforms focus on technology and business capabilities, while small and medium-sized suppliers in tier 3-4 cities or tier 4-5 cities provide labour resources.

Q: According to your observation, has there been a significant change in the budget [ORG] of single clients for data labelling? How much has the budget of some fast-growing industries increased?

[PERSON]: I don’t think there are obvious changes in client budgets for speech and image data labelling services. In comparison, for text data-based projects, the clients will offer higher prices and have more budgets. I think it has something to do with the successful application of [ORG] in some commercial scenarios, making many companies attach great importance to this business. It’s hard to determine the increase rate in budgets because this year is not over yet and the overall change remains unclear. Considering that players in the industry are optimistic about the prospects of the applications in commercial scenarios, the budget is estimated to increase by about 30-50%.

Q: Today’s Interview mainly focuses on the development status quo and future growth potential of [ORG]. Could you brief us on its establishment background and development routes? What are the major milestones for its growth?

[PERSON]: [ORG] was founded in 2016. In the very beginning, it mainly provided TTS (text to speech) services, which can convert text into speech and play it out. Based on this business, virtual humans with different tones were developed later. It has one product called Dinosaur Beck (恐龙贝克). [ORG] has always focused on the TTS business which involves addressing many technical issues, and it has provided TTS services for many well-known clients. In 2018-19, [ORG] started to provide data collection and labelling service based on the TTS business  line. The service is essentially different from TTS. This Interview mainly focuses on data collection and labelling. In the early days, [ORG] was mainly engaged in providing speech-related services, such as keyword spotting and ASR (automatic speech recognition) data collection and labelling. Since 2020, [ORG] has intended to go public, so it hopes to expand its revenue size. Although [ORG] ranks among the top in China in terms of TTS technology competencies, the TTS market size is relatively small. Therefore, [ORG] hopes to increase its revenue from data collection and labelling. In 2020, [ORG] aimed to start working on image data on the basis of speech data. However, as [ORG] did not have its own data labelling platforms at the time, its clients were less confident in its image data service. In 2022, [ORG] also launched its own data labelling platform and started setting foot in the autonomous driving sector. [ORG] started with the TTS technology. Its data collection and 
labelling services brought revenue, and the image data service significantly boosted its revenue. [PERSON], a well-known doctor of the [ORG] in the ASR sector, joined [ORG] and improved its technical capability, which is a milestone of [ORG]’s development. [PERSON] also redefined [ORG]. It hopes to turn all ASR and TTS technologies into a platform-based free market where all small and medium-sized companies can choose their own technologies at will and transform the technologies into commodities.

Q: Could you elaborate on [ORG]’s development status quo? What is the overall revenue size? What do you think of its growth rate over the past few years?

[PERSON]: I joined [ORG] in 2020 when its overall revenue was about RMB 20m-30m. Its revenue increased to RMB 40m-50m in 2021 and RMB 50m-60m in 2022. The growth rate in H1 2023 may slow down, and the revenue is expected to remain around RMB 60m, which is the same as last year’s. [ORG] has achieved a fast growth rate over the past few years mainly due to the rapid expansion of the data collection and labelling business. [ORG] mainly focused on speech data in 2020-21, and its revenue size increased significantly. It also adopted some marketing strategies and occupied a large market share. In 2022, [ORG] launched its own image annotation platform and seized a market share in the image annotation market; thus, its revenue went up significantly. [ORG] originally planned to use the autonomous driving sector as the main revenue driver this year, but it seems that the sales performance has not been satisfactory. Therefore, [ORG]’s overall revenue growth rate in 2023 may be lower than that over the past few years.

Q: What is the revenue proportion of [ORG]’s products and services? Which products and services will drive [ORG]’s growth in the future, and which sectors that [ORG] has been investing in and planning are you optimistic about?

[PERSON]: [ORG] gets its revenue from three major sources. The first is the data labelling and collection service, which is called the data business. The second source is the TTS business, which is mainly about providing customised tones to clients, including some pronunciation dictionaries. The third source is the technical products based on the data and TTS businesses, including virtual human and speech transcription systems for events such as conferences. The data business contributes 50-60% of the total revenue, while the TTS business about 30%. Technical products contribute the remaining 10%. [ORG] has rolled out its own [ORG]-like platform, which I think will have a large market size in the future, based on the APIs (application programming interface) of other companies. [ORG] can integrate its own ASR and TTS systems with the platform. In this case, users can speak directly to the language model instead of typing. What it returns is no longer a string of words but voices. I think [ORG] is more likely to achieve growth in this aspect in the future. As for the data business, it seems 
that the growth rate of the autonomous driving sector planned at the beginning of this year is relatively slow. Therefore, the target may be unable to be achieved.

Q: [ORG] has completed the latest round of fundraising recently. It mentioned that the main investment direction is large models. What do you think of its competencies in large models and its future development with a relatively complete industry chain?

[PERSON]: [ORG] has raised RMB 100m from its series B round. To be honest, this is not enough in terms of large model training. [ORG] raised USD 10bn to launch [ORG], and it is impossible for [ORG] to do so with RMB 100m. Therefore, it’s basically impossible for [ORG] to develop a new large language model. However, according to previous experience, the market size will
