Such a market test may have little effect in general.

Q: Do you think [ORG] will cooperate with leading AI companies in this regard? After all, they are also its clients.

[PERSON]: I don’t think so. The government has issued a policy stipulating that all technology companies that develop AI models are responsible for all legal responsibilities imposed by AI or even everything exported by AI. If [ORG] cooperates with its client, it needs to provide its clients with the APIs of the source model and AIGC (AI generated content) models. However, I don’t think [ORG] has the ability to bear such a huge legal responsibility or conduct technical reviews on its clients. On the contrary, to develop products based on the API of the source model provided by clients is not in line with [ORG]’s background. [ORG] is a company that sells technologies rather than physical or virtual products. If [ORG] launches a tangible product, such as chatbots, people would think the company is quite awesome because the company isn’t specialising in this field. I think this is where the problem lies I think it is unlikely for [ORG] to cooperate with clients in AIGC. However, due to the rich experience in data services, personnel management, project management and labelling base construction, [ORG] is likely to cooperate with its clients by providing them with mature management systems, and even helping them build labelling teams and platforms.

Q: What is the charging model of [ORG]’s products and services? What is the charging level for different projects?

[PERSON]: As an intermediary platform provider, [ORG] charges for project mainly on a daily basis. It charges RMB 180-200 per person per day for all projects. If [ORG] needs to assign project managers for projects, such as the outsourcing of labelling bases, it may need to factor in management personnel, and property management fees, which increases the charge by RMB 10-20 to about RMB 200-220 per person per day. When charging by the project, the charge is about RMB 100-200 per person per day. [ORG] breaks the project down in line with the difficulty level of the project and charges based on the unit rate.

Q: Does [ORG] adopt an advance payment model or schedule a payback period according to project progress?

[PERSON]: Few clients would pay in advance in the data labelling industry. Most of clients pay within one month after the project is completed. For large-[ORG] or long-term projects, such as projects that last one year, the payment will be made on a monthly basis. Generally speaking, [ORG] charges by the project.

Q: What is the average payback period for most projects?

[PERSON]: The average period is one month after the project is completed.

Q: How long is the project cycle?

[PERSON]: The cycles of different projects vary. For speech and text projects, the average cycle is about 1-2 months, while for image projects, the average cycle may be longer, which is about three months.

Q: You said earlier that the demand for and the business size of data labelling in the autonomous driving sector are growing rapidly. What do you think of [ORG]’s competencies in this regard? Will some clients in the industry prefer [ORG]’s products instead of setting up its own data labelling platform? Or are you more optimistic about other players in the industry?

[PERSON]: [ORG] finished building its own autonomous driving labelling platform in mid-2022 and started to communicate with clients on the platform to see if there were any orders. However, no desirable results were delivered. [ORG]’s employees have also made analysis, showing that there are mainly two reasons behind it. The first reason is that it is not the best time for suppliers to enter the market because most clients already have their own suppliers that have been providing them with data services. Clients have no reason to change their suppliers, as they have already worked together for a long time. Therefore, it is difficult for suppliers to enter the market at this time. The second reason is that ASR, which [PERSON] also specialises in, is [ORG]’s main and most outstanding business. [ORG] hasn’t obtained many technologies or connections in the vision field. Therefore, it has no advantages when negotiating with clients.Therefore, I think [ORG] has neither gained complete market recognition nor the right opportunity to showcase its capabilities in the autonomous driving sector. It is still waiting for a good opportunity to win clients’ confidence and to be trusted with more challenging and urgent orders. If [ORG] performs well in order fulfilment, more clients would like to choose its products and services. I think [ORG], [ORG] and [ORG] are doing pretty well in this sector. Newcomers, such as [ORG] and [ORG], are noteworthy, for they have gained a certain market share in the autonomous driving sector and demonstrated their capabilities to clients.

Q: Could you comment on the current development of other players that you are familiar with?

[PERSON]: [ORG] is one of the earliest image data service providers in China. [ORG] and [ORG] are the two earliest data service providers in China. [ORG] focuses on speech data and it started to develop its business in the autonomous driving sector last year, which is similar to [ORG]. By comparison, [ORG] has always been focusing on providing image data services. It used to be involved in an invasion of privacy, which made it disappear from the industry for some time. However, as an early image data service provider, it still has a high client reach rate in the industry and provides services mainly for state-owned enterprises (SOEs). In addition, it has cooperated with large auto manufacturers in the autonomous driving industry as it is an early player in the industry with a large business [ORG] and extensive experience in this regard. In this sense, it has cooperated with almost all auto manufacturers 
and will provide services for auto manufacturers that are satisfied with its services continuously. Therefore, it will become a well-performing autonomous driving data service provider in China.[ORG], another early data service provider in the industry, focuses on price advantages. It is a data service provider in China adopting a crowdsourcing model, so it has obvious advantages in this regard. It was troublesome for [ORG] to adopt a crowdsourcing model at the early stage because the labelled data may not be qualified due to the lack of training of data labellers. Its platform cannot automatically detect the unqualified labelled data, thus making human task necessary. In this sense, although the labelled data is cheap, the time required to check and modify the labelled data is 2-3 times that required to label the data. Therefore, the overall cost may not be low. However, [ORG] has been conducting business with the crowdsourcing model for many years. In this case, it has formed a significant cost advantage in this regard given its present platform technology and mature personnel and management model. Therefore, [ORG] has a price advantage in autonomous driving data services. Auto manufacturers with insufficient budgets may prefer to cooperate with [ORG]. Moreover, I think [ORG] is also a well-performing player in the industry. As a foreign-funded company with a strong technology background, it has been providing data services globally for many years thanks to its mature platform. [ORG]’s branch in China leverages [ORG]’s technology competencies and provides mature data platforms. In addition, the senior executives responsible for the operations of [ORG]’s branch in China used to work at [ORG], so they have deep connections with well-known domestic companies. Although [ORG]’s branch in China was founded late, its technology has developed rapidly thanks to the technology background in the overseas market. In addition, with a wide range of businesses, it enjoys rapid development momentum in China and is a data service provider with mature technologies and manpower. The only problem with [ORG]’s branch in China is that it is a subsidiary of a wholly foreign-owned company, so it may not be able to participate in the bidding to cooperate with some SOEs as it doesn’t have the inherent conditions for winning the bid. Putting aside these factors, [ORG] is a strong competitor in the industry. [ORG] and [ORG] are two emerging players in the industry that are worth noting. [ORG] is a very patient player. As a latecomer, it doesn’t have many advantages in doing business, but it used to take two years to develop its data labelling platforms. Before [ORG], all the data service providers regard their data platforms as a tool and just focus on the fundamental functions of these platforms. The platforms just play a similar role as hoes and shovels that can help people finish their work in a convenient and fast way. However, [ORG] upgraded the hoes and shoves to electric pickaxes. It added functions such as data statistics and automatic labelling to its platform and designed a beautiful UI, which is similar to the patterns of the electric pickaxes and impresses its clients. In this sense, its clients may think that the platform is completely different from before. In this way, it outperforms other players. Although it was founded late and does not have absolute advantages in business and technology competencies, it gains a foothold in the industry by developing its platform with patience. [ORG] is a data service provider founded in 2022 and its development perfectly shows why we say China is a society of human connections. Since its establishment last year, [ORG] has maintained a small team of around 10 employees. In addition, it has been using platforms developed by others as its own platform is still under development and has not been officially launched. Besides, it has only built close relationships with 1-2 suppliers. However, [PERSON], CEO of [ORG], has been engaged in the automotive industry for a long time. Therefore, [ORG] can acquire many orders through Mr Yu’s personal connections. With these orders, he can develop the platform and manage his team to develop [ORG] in other aspects. 

Q: How relevant are [ORG]’s businesses of autonomous driving data, image data or other data types to its speech data business? Can [ORG] and [ORG] continue to leverage their competencies or knowledge accumulated in the speech data sector? 

[PERSON]: From the perspective of technology, I think the reuse rate of competencies or knowledge 
accumulated in the speech data sector is low. However, from the perspective of project management, speech data projects and image data projects are the same. All these projects of different data types are labour-intensive projects. The management of labour-intensive projects and a large number of personnel is an important part of such projects. Therefore, more than 90% of project management experience, excellent project management personnel and accumulated knowledge related to project management can be reused. Except for different demand for different data types, management models and methods have not changed. 
However, technology competencies have changed. With the development in the data labelling industry, the clients have higher requirements for data service providers other than low labour costs and a large number of employees. Instead, they pay more attention to the technologies adopted and the prelabelling function added to the links of data entry, as well as the elaborate designs of the data platforms to save costs and improve efficiency. In this sense, the platforms for speech data and image data are different in terms of the different technologies adopted. Therefore, although data service providers such as [ORG] and [ORG] intended to enter the image data sector very early, they started largescale business development in this sector around last year. It may take some time for them to accumulate the technologies required for image data or autonomous driving data business.

Q: Which players in the image data sector or in other data type sectors will remain competitive or are you optimistic about? Or will the competitive landscape be more fragmented? Will these players be affected as downstream clients are continuously developing their own data labelling capacity?

[PERSON]: Recently, I am studying the competition between online car-hailing service providers in 2012, especially the merger of [ORG] and [ORG], which is similar to the competition in the data labelling industry today. Many players in the industry have emerged. In addition to well-known large players such as [ORG], [ORG] and [ORG], there are also less renowned players developing data labelling businesses in local places. Moreover, there are smaller human resource outsourcing companies in tier 4-5 cities that also do such businesses in the industry. 
In general, I think there will be a player in the data labelling industry that can dominate the market by merging with other players, playing the same role as that of [ORG] in the online car-hailing service industry. The current situation is that all players are trying to reduce their prices and costs to compete with others, which is similar to [ORG] and [ORG] that provided many subsidies for their users during the competition at that time. The main reason is that there are not many technology barriers in the data labelling industry, and it is difficult to form tough technology barriers. Technically, no matter how well-designed and intelligent a data labelling platform is, just like that of [ORG], the price is the clients’ core procurement criteria. If 
the efficiency of the platform is very high, the price should be low. However, if the platform is low in quality, but the labour cost is very cheap, the final price is still low. There is no way to turn technology advantages of players into technology barriers, therefore, players try their best to compete with others in this regard. I think it’s similar to the situation in the online car-hailing service industry at that time. With thousands of drivers, any company can develop its business like [ORG] even in a county. I think there will be 3-4 players left, and at last, there will be only 1-2 players after the merger between them. The remaining players may have the following characteristics. First, they need to have strong technology competencies or backgrounds, including competencies in terms of their platforms and algorithms. In addition to data labelling, players need to conduct model-based testing in the future, which requires a strong technology background. In addition, they need to have a large business [ORG]. Only by expanding the business [ORG] can the players accumulate more project management experience and summarise quality inspection methods and project processes suitable for data labelling services. With the accumulated experience, they can develop perfect data labelling processes and optimise their platforms, which will gradually form an industry standard. As a result, technology background and enough project volume are two important things. Moreover, they need to have their own data labelling bases. It seems that the outsourcing model is unstable for the players. In this sense, the players rely more on the supply chain. Instead, they need to 
control the supply chain by itself. In a nutshell, I think there will be 3-4 players with all of these capabilities, and at last, they will merge into 1-2 giants.

Q: Is there anything else that has not been covered? Or do you have anything else to share with us? You may also make a summary.

[PERSON]: I think [ORG] is a technology-oriented player that mainly provides speech data services. It cannot be regarded as a data service provider exactly. The biggest difference between [ORG] and other data service providers is that it has a complete technological team and a complete team responsible for the development of algorithms. Other data service providers may have some employees responsible for algorithms but they usually apply open-source algorithms to their own platforms. Perhaps there are only 1-2 employees responsible for this while [ORG] has a team responsible for the self-development of algorithms, which means that it has a very strong technology background. However, [ORG]’s biggest disadvantage is that it pays more attention to speech data services as the technology of speech data is well known to people. However, [ORG]’s development restricted in this way. When it negotiates with companies doing business in the autonomous driving sector, they may think that [ORG] focuses more on speech data rather than image data and they don’t think it can perform well in providing image data services. Instead, they may prefer to cooperate with [ORG], whose capabilities have been proved in this regard.At last, I would like to make a summary of the development of the industry. I think the development in the data labelling industry is closely related to that of AI products. Autonomous driving is a typical application scenario of AI training data labelling at present, so there is greater demand for data labelling 
in the autonomous driving sector. When the ASR of [ORG] was very accurate 4-5 years ago, the demand for speech data labelling was large as ASR is a good application scenario for speech data labelling. Nowadays, as [ORG] becomes the most popular application scenario, there will definitely be greater demand for text data services.

Q: Could you start by describing the current state of the market for machine learning and AI vendors? How would you categorise or segment the core products?

[PERSON]: As a first start, from the overall market where it’s at, obviously investment in tech right now is an interesting space given investment yields and where the tech industry is overall. In terms of a brief overview of what the different vendors in this space look like, I typically think of them in two major buckets. One is data science in a box, so to speak, so point solutions that have AI or say that they have some sort of AI data science product built in within it. That would be something like a martech vendor selling you some sort of consumer 
prediction stuff or health tech services where they’re selling a specific model to their customers, but what’s happening inside of it is really a black box or an algorithm that’s owned and is part of the solution itself vs AI ML development platforms. That’s where it’s really a tool that enables a company to build such products themselves and to leverage their IP, their people, their data to build and develop the solutions. At the highest level, that’s where I think about the two bifurcations of the market. Then when you look at more specifically in that platform space, then it really splits between is it something that is a point solution along that development chain of a data product or is it an end-to-end platform? End-to-end is sometimes an overused term, but thinking about it from data to production. That’s at the highest level 
how I think about the market.

Q: Could you expand on how you broke down the core market segments across the key vendors in the space? Who are the main players? Some players might overlap in multiple areas, such as the hyperscalers, as well as [ORG], [ORG], [ORG], [ORG], [ORG], [ORG] and [ORG]. Who are the market leaders?

Initially, you would see things that were very data-specific focused, so companies like Trifacta or Datameer, most of which have been acquired by now, but were pieces that were just focused on the data prep. This was especially big over the last few years as big data became more common, but as the data has all moved to being in the cloud, the hyperscalers have really taken over ownership of that space when there are single points there. Two more pieces that I’m seeing a lot of interest in, in these point solutions these days, and where I’d say there are more newer start-ups, so one would be in the data labelling space. As companies are using more complex things like computer vision, whether image recognition, video recognition or natural language processing, dealing with unstructured text, stuff like that, companies with large data sets often find that the biggest challenge is the labelling of that data. An example of that being when you’re talking about computer vision for self-driving cars, how do you take that video or that [ORG] input and say, “This is a tree, this is a person. This is a lane, this is a traffic light,” etc. The typical way to do that was of course then have people go through thousands of images and label things, that this is a tree, a car, a person, etc. That’s a limiting factor. Often that would get outsourced to things like [ORG] or offshore places, and you had to deal with some of the pollution and the human biases in that. It’s then just limiting from a cost and time perspective. There are a few companies, [ORG] being the one that I’m the most familiar, but there are others in this space that are developing things which can do automatic labelling. Based on the smaller set, go through and then actually create and tag this stuff automatically. It’s very much a problem for the subset of companies/use cases where you’re dealing with massive data. This tends to be most in that unstructured space, so like I said, text, combing stuff off the internet or looking through chat logs or things like that or image and video where you just have a lot of data that needs to be labelled for semantics or image recognition, etc. That’s one space where there is still a lot of development, and I’m seeing companies growing in that space. There’s still interest there. Like I said, it’s very much a small subset of problems that companies who are doing AI actually face, and I would even say, to some extent, is biased a little bit by the tech industry trying to solve tech problems for itself. is an area of a lot of investment is I think typically referred to as ML ops. That tends to honestly be some of the biggest challenge of putting an AI or a data product into production is actually that once you know what the solution is, how do you actually make use of it on a day-to-day basis? Q: Large language models using natural language processing models such as GPT-3 and [ORG] technology have seen tremendous interest in 2022. [PERSON]: Especially the language generation side of things, the most obvious there is for the next generation of the chatbot, so to speak, of how do you replace your customer service and your standard customer interactions with an AI that’s going to be able to do that at a lower cost than doing it with humans. That, to me, is the first and most obvious use case. much that I think it is going to provoke much more interest in regulation than anything had before. Trademarks, infringement of patents and there are so many things that now suddenly became very acute challenges. I think regulation is going to come to that market much, much faster than we’ve seen it react to most of the AI and data science space. [PERSON]: It’s a good question. I think that the market’s going to continue to accelerate in terms of the spend. My favourite anecdote there was working for a CEO a number of years ago who when first was introduced to me, knew I did data science, said, “We should be doing more of that data science.” I said, “We’ve got hundreds of systems from dozens of acquisitions that the data’s all over the place. We need to fix the data before we can go do these things.” He said, “Let’s go do data science.” I think that there are still people who have that mindset. It certainly will move faster. Nobody’s experimenting with building Hadoop onprem. Nobody’s trying to figure out what this big data thing is. How fast that moves is really the question for me, and I think probably what affects the variation and most of those numbers you looked at, but I think it’s easily, to me, a USD 500bn market by the end of the decade at the latest, and probably even sooner.

of a customer to develop and deploy data products on the hyperscalers. In the other, the smaller companies, [ORG] even, while they’re ahead on this curve a little bit, there still needs to be more service providers who are focusing on building a practice to enable using these tools before they’re really going to be able to see the spread that’s necessary. One of the most interesting things I saw, I think it was in the last year, was a report by [ORG] which actually looked at the services-to-software ratio for data science vendors, rather than your typical SaaS vendors. It found that instead of that 90 to 10 software-to-services revenue that they actually saw it was often more in like a 60-70 to 30-40 ratio where it was actually a lot more services necessary to enable companies to be successful with data science. While it hasn’t quite yet changed the actual way that data science companies are being evaluated by the VCs and PEs, I think that that will continue to happen. It’s why [ORG] and [ORG], when you look at the most recent [ORG], not the Magic [ORG] but whatever their intermediate before going to the next Magic [ORG] is, had those as the leaders is because of the services behind them. [ORG] sits slightly in the same bucket, but [ORG]’s product is so incoherent that I actually have yet to hear of a commercial entity being successful leveraging [ORG]. I actually get a lot of customers who were [ORG] users and then gave up on it because they call it [ORG], but it’s really 30-something different products behind the scenes that are completely disjointed and you can’t really work with. I rarely even think about them, but [ORG] and [ORG] are still so services heavy that they’re going to be more successful in enabling a new company that doesn’t have that capability already itself.

Q: Can you explain the importance of synthetic data? A [ORG] study projected that 60% of all data used in AI development will be synthetic vs real by 2024. [ORG] launched a synthetic data platform called [ORG]. How will synthetic data create new opportunities across industries?

[PERSON]: I’ll start by saying it’s not an area that is a specialty for me, so it’s not one that is probably my strongest area to opine on. The thing that I will say, the unforeseen challenge that I see there is that synthetic data still typically comes from a seed or looking at the existing data and just attempts to randomise and replicate at a [ORG] that allows for some of the large transformer or GPT type models to be able to actually be functional. It’s still based on the initial data that you started with, just blended up and then made bigger. That to me, the challenge there that we’ll really see is that I think the first attempts to really build and (? 42.35) models on this synthetic data will rather than giving you a larger normalised data set that you can acquire otherwise, actually suffers from potentially magnifying the biases or the challenges implicit in the initial data set. I’m actually really sceptical about it personally. Again, I’m not 
technically in the depths enough to be able to say for certain, but I think that the first wave of this synthetic data will actually probably fail spectacularly is my guess, or the models built on the synthetic data. Where it goes after that I think may make sense. I was reading something this morning, even just thinking about building natural language models, these large language models or these large image generation models, it’s now already to a point where you can’t get data off the internet in the way that you used to, knowing that it was human-generated and not AI generated because there’s already so much out there. As it gets better, we were talking about [ORG] or some of the image… Like [ORG] or [ORG] and those things. The next generation of those models will be fed on AI-generated data and will then continue to exacerbate the biases and challenges or gaps that were in the data to begin with that the model picked up on and then magnifies. I actually think that we’re almost going to end up having this synthetic data experiment because of the amount of data going into the internet especially that is now synthetically generated by these large models that we’re going to see weird things happening in the next generations of these models. Now it’s just going to an AI feeding itself its own data, and then moving to a next generation. I’m very sceptical, I think that there are actually going to be some significant challenges with that that’s going to cause some serious course correction in how these models are being developed.

Q: How important are TCO [total cost of ownership] and ROI considerations when customers are evaluating the use of AI and machine learning vendors? What other evaluation criteria is there? How might evaluation criteria change amid a potential recession in 2023?

[PERSON]: I’d say it was already becoming more important. I think part of it is just the maturity stage of the market. Companies like where I was at 10 years ago that said, “This is a thing that seems important and we should go invest some money in it,” and I had a budget over the course of a couple of years to just try things out. If it didn’t work, it didn’t work, but we were deliberately being innovative and trying new platforms, that’s far less likely today. Companies that are going to market on these things now have either been at it long enough that they know what they’re spending their money on, and they already have the numbers in hand to know what they’ve been successful with, and they know what they’re looking for going forward. Even more so, companies that are now in the early stages of maturity of trying to adopt these tools, the so-called pragmatists from that technology adoption curve, they’re driven far more by the numbers in the ROI than the early adopters and the people who were trying things out were. That was a trend that was already really happening, I’d say, over the last… Especially the last 12 months, but maybe the last 2-3 years is where it’s slowly hit that tipping point into people who are thinking far more about the ROI. I think that the recession, if there’s a recession, that that will drive just that change faster. I saw it coming anyway that boards and c-suites that have been funding these things over the last few years, the vast majority of revenue or real return on investment that has been captured has been captured by a small subset of the companies investing here.I sometimes say pejoratively that a lot of times, companies are paying a consulting company or paying for a vendor to be able to put in their 10-K that they’re doing AI because it makes the investors happy, but they’re not actually doing anything other than just buying a solution. They’re not really actually investing in it themselves. To some extent, I don’t think boards and c-suites knew any better, to be super honest. They don’t have knowledge in this. Your average board member or your average c-suite member has very little understanding of what the actual data science and the AI capabilities look like. Outside of tech and even sometimes in tech, it’s less knowledge than you would assume. I think that it was already 
coming where boards are asking tough questions. C-suites are asking tough questions saying, “We spent USD 50m or USD 100m over the last three years on all these data scientists and these platforms and everything. What do we have for our money?” A recession is only going to intensify that conversation. I think sometimes, that will end up cutting off the true R&D side of data science, where sometimes you don’t know if the model or the project is going to work, and you have to take those big swings. Those are both riskier but tend to have the bigger opportunity, and it’ll focus more on maybe… They’re sometimes called the low-hanging fruit type models and projects and investments, which in a recession certainly feels safer, but I think the companies that do pull back too much will end up falling behind postrecession vs the companies that continue to invest in it. I think investment will continue to happen. I think we’ll see it accelerate in some companies that are maybe a little further ahead or just have the right mix of people there, and then companies that are already a little bit behind will pull back on their spending, focus more on the ROI. It’ll be too uncertain, and then they’ll probably exit and just end up falling further behind. I think we even saw that a little bit, really, over the last couple of years with COVID, where some companies put their foot on the gas at the beginning of COVID investing in some of these capabilities and have seen huge returns on that. I certainly 
see that with some of my customers vs companies that either disappeared or companies that were in the market that went off the market. Now they’re back at the table again and probably going to walk away from the table again if a recession happens. I think it will cause actually a lot of differentiation there.

Q: What are the impacts of tech industry lay-offs on the machine learning industry? It seems there’s a lot of prominent data science talent floating around. Are you seeing any vendors snatching up this talent?

[PERSON]: Honestly, in the tech space, not yet. All of the folks that I am talking to in tech, really every sort of stage of maturity in tech is really on pause right now. Other than a handful of companies that are continuing to invest because they are already pretty big, most of the companies have pulled back. I’m really seeing that all the way from series A to public tech companies where this is happening. Tech overall, with maybe a couple of exceptions, and honestly [ORG] hired a lot through COVID, I haven’t heard that they’re slowing their stuff down yet, but that’s one of the few that really had kept their foot on the gas over the last couple of years anyway. It really does feel… everybody was paused at least through Q4 and early into next year, waiting to see what happens. Where most of that talent is going is to the commercial side. I think that that’s going to be a really interesting development of this is that the… It’s called working in the industry, working in finance or working in retail or manufacturing or whatever, it’s been so hard for those companies to hire good data science talent for so long because it was really almost like a just, “We’re going to hire all the talent to stay in tech. We don’t even know how we’re going to use it, but we’re just going to keep ahead by continuing to hire this talent.” It’s really made it hard for some of the data scientists to move over to the industry side and hard for those industry people to get good talent. I think that that’s going to shift rapidly. I was certainly talking to some of our customers in the commercial space...

Q: Are vendors doing a good job at getting traction and expanding use cases for current customers? What challenges do vendors face when approaching this?

[PERSON]: I talked a little bit about it earlier in terms of the services side tends to be higher in the data science companies. I think that that will continue to be a real differentiator for the companies because I think while we are seeing this diffusion of talent from tech into industry, there’s a learning curve there. Even in my experience when I was in the industry of hiring people who had come from tech, it was very rare that they were plug-and-play successful right off the bat. It took time for them to learn the industry, to get into the use cases, all of that. The gap there, even if this spread of talent from tech continues, that gap really still needs to be filled by either the tech vendors themselves or by their partners who do have that industry knowledge. You certainly see stuff like [ORG] leaning very heavily into having industryspecific teams, verticalised teams, that can deliver these sorts of use cases. [ORG], before their current challenges, had already been in that model for a long time, and I think where a lot of their early success came from was delivering on the use cases. The challenge there was they weren’t often enabling customers to deliver the use cases, and I think that that’s where this real challenge lies is you get more success earlier by helping a customer productionalise a use case, but the long-term success is by helping companies do that themselves because that’s then how your platform actually grows. I think that that sort of enablement is a big gap there. Even when you 
look at the partners, it’s the same thing. If you bring in a [ORG], the [ORG]’s first inclination is to sell you people to build a project for you, not to sell you people to train you how to do that product yourselves. Beyond just a data science boot camp or that sort of stuff, but teaching companies how to do this themselves is really probably the biggest gap. The companies that can do that the best will, in the long term, be the most successful. That’s what’s going to grow their platform. It’s not going to be, “I’m going to come build five models for you.” It’s going to be, “I’m going to come teach 10,000 people at your company to build models, and they’re going to go build five million models.” That’s how you actually [ORG].

Q: You mentioned tech cost-cutting and reduction of speculative R&D spend in the industry. What are the margin expansion opportunities for the mature machine learning and data science vendors?

[PERSON]: I think the area that I said earlier that I think is the biggest untapped market is really the less technical users in companies. I really think that that’s where the true growth is going to come from. When you look at a company that is really doing data science at [ORG], and there are very few companies that are doing this, but the companies that are doing data science bigger, it’s not that they have thousands of data scientists. They certainly have a higher proportion, but it’s that those data scientists are enabling a larger share of the company to work at. Any company that has knowledge workers, if a company has 10,000 knowledge workers, a company that has a ton of data scientists might have 500 out of that 10,000. The offerings in the hyperscalers, especially with the [ORG], Dominos, etc, most of the offerings have always been focused on that 500 data scientists. Flipping to being able to serve products for the other 9,500 knowledge workers at a company like that, that, to me, is the biggest part of the market that is still out there to be attained. By no means is that market even remotely tapped yet. There’s still a ton of growth to be had there.

Q: You mentioned industry consolidation trends and areas that are ripe for M&A. Which areas of the market might start seeing M&A activity most quickly?

[PERSON]: It’s a good question. I think that the auto ML, to me, that’s one. That really hasn’t proved to be a standalone product that’s really sustainable, so I think that that’s the first one I see. The second pieces that I see then for the auto data labelling and the ML ops, because those are both the two areas that have had the most investment over the last couple of years, but there are also a lot of products in both those spaces that are still early days, and I think it’s going to happen whereas when you look at the more mature, the broader platform spaces, lots of companies collapsing into a few has already happened. That’s why that M&A space for these younger nascent areas, and maybe even the language generation stuff, [ORG], [ORG], some of these pieces you mentioned earlier, there are a lot of those vendors, there are a handful that have made bigger names, but even those are all still pretty young. That would be 
another space because there’s so much interest there. How regulation, and if it really starts to look like regulation’s going to accelerate there, could very much impact the long-term prospects of those companies and the likelihood of M&A.

Q: Do you have any concluding thoughts regarding the machine learning industry?

[PERSON]: I think that the last piece that I would just think through is just the I don’t think that anything is going to materially change over the next few years, given the current market and the potential recession and investment and everything like that. I think it’s accelerating some of the changes that would have happened, but we may end up where we would have been five years from now in 2-3 years. I don’t think it’s fundamentally changing the market, it’s just changing timelines.

[PERSON]: Thank you so much for joining us today. It’s been a great conversation. Thank you, clients, for joining [ORG]’s Interview.

[PERSON]: My pleasure, Jordan. Thank you everybody for attending. Thank you for having me

Buyers: These are typically organizations or research institutions that require AI training data to develop or enhance their machine learning models. Screening Questions
Can you address the typical purchasing process for AI training data (e.g. who are the main stakeholders and how long does the process take)? Provide a brief 1-2 sentence explanation.
Yes. The typical purchasing process for AI training data involves several key stakeholders, including data scientists who assess the quality and relevance of the data, procurement managers who negotiate pricing and manage transactions, and legal teams who ensure data privacy compliance. The process duration varies depending on data complexity and quantity but can span weeks to months, encompassing data identification, vetting, negotiation, procurement, and integration into AI systems.

What does the purchasing process of AI training data look like? Who are the main stakeholders and how long does the process take?
" The main stakeholders are the Researchers and Developers who need to specify the data that they need, and the Legal Department to ensure that the data acquired allows for publication of results and productization of the trained models. Budgets are typically set head of time, but can be adjusted. The time to purchase depends greatly on the regulations that are involved, the methods of generating or acquiring thee data (if labeled data is needed), and the amount of the data required. If labeled data is needed for supervised learning, generation and acquisition of the data can take months, but the purchasing is uallay measured in weeks."

Autonomous mobility - includes autonomous vehicles, drones, etc 
Retail self-checkout and loss prevention

NLP: With the release of Chat GPT and other LLMs, now the challenge is to improve domain-specific applications of these capabilities. This will start with medical AI/healthtech and legal markets. [PERSON]: Sure. I kind of boil it down to 3 areas. 1 is context. So it allows us an algorithm to identify actions, objects, relationships between objects in a scene. There’s not a great kind of general model out there that works for thousand of different actions but there’s a lot of research going into these areas that are really going to expand the capabilities down the road. And then object persistence, so be able to aggregate activity associated with a specific object over time. So in a self driving example, when we’re driving down he road two minutes in there there’s a blue cargo van that comes into frame and moves out and then being able to understand two minutes later that when that blue cargo van comes back that it’s still the same cargo van. Or in sports when you have players that are involved in different plays, optics persistence means that this player four on the red team and not having to reestablish that on every image. And so beyond that we see a lot of growth in human pose estimation and this is really a specific flavor of computer vision that allows us to build applications that do more than just track objects. Q: What opportunities were created from the Covid-19 pandemic for using an application video data?

A: So we’ll take retail as an example, early on you see actual big retailers building their own AI solutions. They have AI teams, they have data science teams. But your local retailer or the smaller chains, almost anybody except for the really big ones is going to have the capacity, really have that competency internally to do that. And so what we see is the growth of point solution players. So we have kind of the self-checkout space, or if you think just traditional checkout space like National Cash Register ([ORG]), [ORG], all these other players, there’s no point solution players, many of them, that are building a full frictionless shopping experience or self checkout. This applies to security monitoring as well. We’re seeing a lot of point solutions that are being built or the end customer isn’t necessarily building it themselves. I say. It’s especially with video and it’s early compared to image which we’re seeing a lot of growth because we’re working a lot of customers that this is their first video project. So in short, we’ve seen that shift in the last six years. Nobody was doing video, well, video in the beginning was people were using things like see that or some other tools to annotate. 

Q: [ORG] has done great research on this market – I know they have over 150 vendors in this market in their last report. What do you see as what separates all these vendors and your competitive advantage?

A: That’s something we’ve seen is the growth over time of the competitive landscape and to be clear, it’s like a football field and we all have a push mower. Like there’s a lot of grass out there. So obviously you look at somebody like [ORG] and they have the definitive stack for three point cloud since our fusion and that’s where they’ve been successful. We’ll talk about where these kind of players are successful. You look at somebody like [ORG] where they have really focused on self service integrating with mlapse teams and that’s been where they’ve had success. [ORG] is focused on enterprise customers and AI companies and so we have structured our products and offering to basically be able to sell to those customers and the man or they want to buy so that’s gonna mean a mix so example would be we have customers who want to use our self service SaaS platform internally with their data science teams when they’re doing research and they maybe have an internal group of 2020 annotators and so they’re able to iterate to do this themselves but when it comes time for the production labeling, they dont want to manage 600 annotators. But that’s easy for us. And so you need all those different elements to be successful. You need to have self-service SaaS and you need full managed services that can put thousands of people on a workflow when you need it. I think the fact that we’re focused on enterprise and across industry, that’s really from a positioning perspective I think where we sit. 

Q: How has data labeling for video data can support the resolution of social problems and problems of social inequality? Have you worked on anything like that? Have you seen anything like that?

A:  I don’t have a specific example on how it’s helped but what I will say is there is an extreme amount of interest in research being done to understand bias in data sets things in video. Yes, there’s things in video that can kind of complicate that and so I wish we worked on something like that that had a clear outcome but I will say that we are really focused internally on making sure our models are unbiased as possible and if something that just needs a continual focus across the industry as well as in Academia.

Q: How does [ORG] label their data for video ML? Is it in-house purchase or something else?

A: So we're an annotation, data labeling company. How it works is that a customer prospect comes with a bunch of videos and a set of annotation requirements, our kind of success people if it’s not self service where they’re doing it themselves, right? They work with the customer success person who takes those annotation requirements, works with our data services team to develop kind of the annotation flow, what stages are going to use, conditional logic, where do we need reviews in the process? And then our data services team curates a workforce. I guess technically if you want a big number, we have up to a million where people that we can put on workflows. But really what this looks like is we curate teams that hopefully have worked on similar data in the past and we get them set up, put them through a training program specific to the workflow that they’re going to be annotating and you train the QC folks and you agree on a review strategy with the customer and we start annotating and they do that in browser. They could be depending on the customer security requirements, they may be home, they may be be at a dedicated work, no cameras right, so it’s very secure. So out of that comes annotation [ORG] data that gets review and a review portal and customers can download it from there.

Q: What are the privacy concerns using video versus images?

A: If nothing else, I think video gives you more context than you can derive more from that I will say we’ve done a lot of work with anonymization so that involves things like identifying text blurring faces, doing it whatever we can to make PII not PII anymore. There’s a lot more of it so you can imagine, think from home security cameras, like how much is visible there? And you can see there’s diplomas on the walls and mail on the counter. So same thing at any of these kind of scenarios where you’re pulling out your credit card or you ID right.

The simple one was led the technical audience leads the development and figure it out and then there was the opposite to that which was like, let’s use commercial talent and business folks to manage AI and I tend to say that just like making soda is different from making beer, making AI is very different from making software right. I think these are all very good developments to deal with the challenge of data as the starting point of any AI project but there’s 2 problems I see to that too: which is 1. We’re starting to be a little bit in an echo chamber with regards to which is the right way to treat data and data mesh is great and I do believe in its concepts and designs but I think the devil is in the details and every company is a little different and you really need to evaluate carefully where it works for you and where it doesn’t, we shouldn’t just be in an echo chamber of saying this method works, this data measures the best and let’s move away from monolithic data lakes right. I think there’s a little bit more that goes into it. 2. The other side that got a little bit missed out is this process management, project management, and just general execution skill set of what it takes to get projects out the door and essentially drive business value and so often times companies would either do their typical waterfall or they would try to repurpose agile software management and make AI projects work and it’s just not a good fit in general. It’s first of all not a good fit in general to go about it this way and second, it just incredibly increases the timeline that it takes to deliver on AI projects. So I think that our ability to now treat the need for good data as its own core asset as well as the need for good MLs its own core asset as well as the need for good operationalization practices for MLs own core asset is going to be fundamental to companies getting better at delivering projects and so it’s an exciting space. I do again as I stated in the beginning, I firmly believe that for data and AI projects that the conversation has to be about what’s the value that we’re driving for the business which in 80-90% of the cases is determined based on financial value. Sometimes there can be value for the business, can come from time, from better and higher efficiency. It can come from example, business value can be extracted also from being good at talent retention, but you really have to be super clear that the main goal of any of these is to drive that business value to have that impact on the business right. And so, that already means that you need to work very closely with the commercial and the business leads together in order to figure out what could be the value from this because a lot of companies don’t even know - okay we want to do a recommender system, we believe if we personalize our experiences better for our customers, it will drive more value, it will drive more customer retention, but quantifying that is a whole different issue and so often times companies revert to very traditional consulting like business financial modeling methodology in order to estimate that. And I think in the beginning if you're at an early stage or a young company that is just starting with this, that’s fair because you need to create a baseline but if you’re a little but farther ahead, I would argue that obviously a-b experimentation is a very industry standard practice. You can even do some benchmark and research and what have other companies in the industry or in similar position seen from these projects get delivered for that top line or for business impact, but it creates this iterative cycle with the commercial teams of figuring out what the value behind these projects is and then at the same time, often times, the technical teams are the data organizations spend a lot of time figuring out what does it actually take for us to do this? And they’re trying to then figure out we have the data if the data is available, how usable is it? We have the models and tools, can we even get it to production, does our app even allow for it from a [ORG] end perspective, are there other software engineering changes we need to make in order to even get this done? There’s a lot of aspects that can go into these frameworks but I think it’s good to at least think in terms of impact and feasibility and timing and then at least at maybe one layer of detail behind each of these right so business impact, you can have financial and efficiency or effectiveness. For feasibility you obviously should have the data aspect in there, you should have the production aspect in there, can you get it in production does your app allow for it? And then for timing it’s really a question of what’s the overall business strategy and could we even deliver this in a timeframe that would work for that shareholder goal that the company has. But the reality is really 50-60% of companies I’ve seen, they even go through that stage.

[PERSON]: Right. We do too. In one of our AI failures podcasts, one of the common reasons that we talked about what we see projects fail is the return on investment, the ROI isn’t there and you’re right, a lot of times people do measure it from a financial standpoint, but we can also look at at it from a time, a resource allocation standpoint as well which all eventually kind of boils down to a financial standpoint and that financial return on investment but people aren’t doing that and I think one thing that maybe folks aren’t also budgeting for is that it’s never a set of forget it type thing, you need to constantly be monitoring, managing especially your models that are out in production as well and earlier you would talk about ML ops. So maybe how have you seen this space of ML ops taking, can you maybe quickly share your definition of ML ops and how have you seen it evolved over the past few years?

[PERSON]: Honestly I think the ML ops space is really just like 5 years old. The worst, obviously, idea around it that is older than that, essentially I think most people would agree that ML ops was coined by a research paper published by a couple of [ORG] folks and I believe in 2015, it’s called hidden technical debt of machine learning. It’s a very famous research paper and in a way it was kind of like the beginning point of ML space. But it took at least 3 or 4 years after that for a lot of companies and startups and practices to start developing around the space of ML ops so specifically in the past 3-4 years, we’ve actually seen an incredible explosion of companies in the space whose goal is to make it easier or better or more efficient or effective to get machine learning models or get AI into production and have it deliver value, right. And so, very quickly, we’ve gotten to a level where there’s tens of thousands of companies and startups actually working in that space very similar to what the martech industry went through in the early 2000s, and then into 2010s. And the difference here is and what we’ve seen in the martech industry too is that none of these companies.. So there’s 2 problems I see with ML ops space at the moment: One is none of these companies has really figured it out 100%, so like there’s a lot of good tooling, a lot of good things available for doing  for example feature engineering or for doing moral selection or for doing modeling deployment or for doing data quality, but they each are still very niche and they only solve 70-80% of that issue. And then you end up with this stack that has for every different step of ML has 2, 3, 4 or 5 different tools. You need to choose in order to make it work and it becomes very complex, right. The second problem I see is that some of these problems with ML ops space are not necessarily in my viewpoint enough of a product differentiator for you to survive as a startup in the long term because the salute, the problem you’re solving, the component you’re solving as part of the ML stack is a very niche problem to solve to begin with and you know a startup that is founded on having some type of an ML or AI technology or some kind of a great trained model that they’re selling by the prediction, that to me is not a very long term stable business model. And so as an executive, I’m also worried about the resilience and the continuity of some of this space and some of these companies and so that makes the ML ops space incredibly difficult too. So we’re still seeing this evolve, I think it’s going to take at least another 2-4 years for us to get to get to the point where it’s been a little bit more solidified out, what are really the key components, who is really great at solving them and how do I then build that stack? And in addition to that, we obviously continue to see the platformization of the whole data approach through companies like [ORG], Snowflakes and even the cloud providers themselves. 

[PERSON]: Obviously we’ve been tracking the market as well and we’ve seen it evolve. You’re right, ML ops is really a thing that you do, right. So you need organizations to understand it, you need organizations to say “okay once I get my model created, what do I do with it?” I have to operationalize it but then I need to have dated governance, model governance, model retraining as necessary and you need to have folks understand that so that you are applying those best practices and you’re making sure that you’re being successful continuously. That’s why we say it’s not a set and forget type of thing. So kind of going along with that, what are some of the most important areas for organizations to focus on in the area of ML ops? Maybe from your experience, especially where have you seen that?

[PERSON]: I think most important right now is monitoring and versioning is really critical. And I mean monitoring and versioning of your data as well as monitoring and versioning of your models in production. Typically those are areas where I think companies are still lagging behind the most and I know a few people in my network are very happy right now that I said that especially some of the startup founders in the monitoring and observability space but the unfortunate truth is also that I don’t think monitoring is necessarily rocket science. It is a complex problem, it takes a lot of attention to detail and a lot of engineering to solve it but is it enough of a differentiator for a business to thrive on this in the long run? Because companies look at this very much still as a make or buy type of problem. And if you choose to buy maybe you get a couple more features, but how valuable for you as a company is that really over investing a couple of months of engineering time to get certain versioning and monitoring practices in place yourself, right? So there’s one example where I say, you know  ML ops is critical monitoring and versioning is probably the most critical component of the entire stack. But no company there has solved it a hundred percent yet, because we are still at this point where I could do it kind of myself, I don’t yet see it. And we’re getting there and I think there are and I think there are some companies out there that have incredible solutions for this space, but I’m curious to see how the integrations of those solutions in the overall stack  continue to be and quite frankly, if I look at a company like [ORG], for example, what would stop [ORG] form just acquiring the missing components of the ML ops stack in order to fulfill the end to end platform need that they have. So a lot of things are happening but yeah monitoring and versioning is a big one both for models and for data because that’s where typically the breakage points are, your models get stale or your data breaks. And so this is where you lose the most time if something goes wrong and if you start losing time, it means you start losing money and that’s when it gets uncomfortable, right. Outside of that, I see a lot of developments in the auto ML space continuing. So just the model selection space we’ve had, I mean [ORG], Dataiku and all of these companies have been around for a while. I think there’s even [ORG] auto ML, [ORG] now has its own [ORG] platform that does some of it, that has been around for a while. But what I actually find much more exciting is for example, a company like [ORG] that isn’t auto ML because you’re still selecting the models that you want to choose to deploy but it’s essentially a form of transfer learning because you use a lot of pre-trained models, a lot of pretend or artifacts and fit it to your business environment in order to make it work and put it in production. And so I think what we’re going to see is over the next couple of years, it’s kind of the merge of this idea of like pre-trained transfer learned models with the auto ML to really start making the selection and deployment of models and production much easier. And so what that means for the role of the data scientists is that it becomes less important how great you are at building models and improving the algorithm and doing these kinds of things in the case of the 80% of businesses, right. Not in a highly customized business context or technical context, but it’s more important to become critical thinkers and knowing how to connect the dots and knowing how to translate business problems into a meaningful way in which you can use your stack to quickly get a model to production and show value from it. So it's a very interesting development at the moment.

Part 2: Q&A

Q: Do you see better tools as a solution to your problem? And if not, what are the solutions to the AI failures?

A: So yes, I do think improvements in tools are, I think that’s just like a cycle that we’re in and cannot escape and it will just continue, right. I think you have to build an infrastructure and the baseline that works for you at least 3-4 years out. But you cannot let yourself fall into the trap of becoming too dependent on one or the other because I think the space is still moving too fast with too many tool improvements that make big steps forward still over the next three years so that you start being locked in. I think maybe in 4-5 years we’re at a point where the opportunity cost of being brought into a lesser set of tools that do things better versus more tools that do a lot of niche things were at that point where we can feel comfortable making that trade-off, but we’re not there yet, right. So that’s number one. Number two, I’d also be very mindful of where I choose the more tools and where I choose to wait, set it out and observe for a little bit longer accepting the challenges difficulties that come with that because for example, monitoring and versioning I think is an area where you’re at a point now where it makes sense for you to start experimenting with some of the tools that are out there and see what works really. Well right, but for example, if you're a standard e-commerce company, or if you’re not a large big tech company with super extreme low latency needs. How complex do you really want to get on the feature store? How complex do you really want to get on data contracts? So those are areas where we say okay, observe a little bit longer or just continue with the tools that kind of do the 60-70% for you now, but don’t necessarily jump the gun and switch now already, right. So it’s not gonna go without tools and it’s actually amazing that there’s so many people and companies out there trying to solve these problems even the small ones and getting better at it incrementally. But we are going to see the platformization and this moving a little bit closer together and a little bit of a shakeout of a lot of these AI and ML companies over the next three years. So I want to be a little bit mindful of that too. 

Q: Aren’t you just chasing your tail? If you think that vendors and tool solutions will solve the problem of AI failures. If even the best technology vendors are seeing massive AI project failures, this isn’t a problem of bad tools.

A: it’s not just a problem of bad tools. I mean the world is not as extreme as that. The solution and the reality is somewhere in that gray zone in between. I think a lot of companies are either knowingly or unknowingly chasing their own tail because of the space that we’re in right now. But you can gain a certain level of comfort in that if you focus on your core priorities, your core projects, your core data assets and do less better rather than more faster. And so you can generate more value from AI and ML to me that is out of the question. But I agree with you that even large tech companies, frankly any company tech or not, is failing in the millions and tens of millions at AI projects and sometimes those failures have been worth it for good learning for knowing what is working and what is not but if you focus on the core, you can drive money and value from it and you can keep observing what is happening in this place over the next 2-3 years and them make more strategic decisions as you go along. But yeah, I think there’s a certain level of chasing your own tail. It’s just unavoidable.  

Q: Where do you see AI going in the next five years?

A: A lot of people ask that question always and I’ll tell you where I think I see Ai going and I think everybody should take anybody saying they see AI is going in the next five years with a huge grain of salt. So take me with a huge grain of salt. But we are certainly going to continue to move away from custom models, in-house build models, like just large teams focusing on improving algorithms and finding the right models towards more transfer learning, more pre-trained, more out of the box people, more out of the box deployments. I also think that the auto ML space has like decreased in attention a little bit over the past 2-3 years and that is just simply for the reason that either we weren’t able to show enough value from the existing technologies and tools or we don’t talk openly enough where we are using it and how we’re using it but I think that we’re going to see a renaissance of that space over the next three to four years that is going to be very specific to the platformization approach where you have companies like [ORG], like Snowflakes and others or even [ORG] Platform providing really good end-to-end means of very quickly getting a use case into production. So I think the ML ops space obviously talked about it a lot like that’s going to keep evolving very strictly and I think it’s going to also mean that we’re going to continue to see changes in what we believe is ML ops and what it is not and which steps fall in here and there and which fall or not. I do think that overall we are going to see more real-time low latency type use cases and deployments more broadly in companies and the industry as that type of machine learning becomes easier and more achievable for companies to buy those improvements in the ML ops space. But I think honestly rather than thinking too much about where we’re going to be in five years, I would encourage a lot of companies to focus on how we can be really great at where we are at now, where we are today, right. How we can make what we have today work and really drive value for us in a meaningful way because there’s a lot that can be done, a lot that is available and a lot of it is a really good stage and I think we need to focus on that rather than continuously trying to figure out if we have reached generalized intelligence in three years, five years, fifteen years or never. 

Final thoughts: I think that the importance of AI and AI applications will continue to grow at an increasing exponential rate. I think the adoption of it will continue to grow but also the challenge and the understanding for it will grow meaning that companies will pay very close attention to the quality and the manner in which AI applications are used as part of the business model because the awareness and education amongst executives and leaders and people in companies is just growing and therefore grows both the ideas in which it can help as well as the understanding of how it cannot help. And so I think I’m very excited for the years ahead. Like I think that data and AI is incredibly important to every business model, but I also think that we need to recognize the complexity of it, be honest with ourselves and really try to find the right solutions, find the right use cases, find the right deployment patterns for that business model for that company that you are working for.
